The Validity of Iqalah After Death of the Subject Matter in Salam
Ruling on iqalah when the received slave-girl dies and the reverse case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqalah when the received capital in a salam contract dies, explain the rationale based on the nature of the subject matter, and apply the ruling to analogous cases.

Core Idea
In salam, iqalah remains valid after the slave-girl dies because the true subject matter is the debt, which survives in the liability.

The classical textbooks often discuss edge cases that illuminate the underlying principles of contract law. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a seemingly specific scenario: a salam contract where the buyer gives a slave-girl as capital, receives her, then the parties rescind, and the slave-girl dies. The ruling and its rationale reveal the Hanafi understanding of iqalah as a rescission of the original obligation, not a return of the specific item. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its implications, including a related dispute on mixing goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Ruling
The matn states: if the slave-girl was taken possession of and the parties rescind (iqalah), then she dies, the rescission 'remains valid and is correct, and upon him is her value.' The opposite case (a regular sale of a slave-girl who dies before iqalah) is invalid. Identify these two cases.
The opposite case is given as 'عكسها شراؤها بألف' – buying her for a thousand, i.e., a regular sale where the sold item is the slave-girl herself.
2
Understand the Rationale
The commentator explains that the condition for iqalah is the continued existence of the subject matter of the original contract. In salam, the subject matter is the debt (musallam fih) owed by the seller, not the specific slave-girl. Since the debt remains in the liability even after the slave-girl dies, the iqalah is valid. The seller must pay the slave-girl's value because he cannot return the specific item.
This distinguishes salam from a regular sale, where the sold item is a specific thing; its destruction voids iqalah.
3
Analyze the Condition for Iqalah
The text clarifies that only the sold item (mabi') must exist for iqalah, not the price. If the price is destroyed, iqalah can still be valid provided there is no discharge (ibra). This is a key principle.
Refer to the quote from al-Qunyah: 'إذا أبرأ البائع المشتري عن الثمن بعد قبض المبيع ثم تقابلا لا تصح' – if seller discharges buyer from price after possession, then iqalah is invalid.
4
Examine the Sub-Issue: Mixing and Possession
The passage transitions to a related case: if the buyer mixes the salam goods with his own property indistinguishably, does this constitute consumption (istihlak) affecting possession? The text presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa considers it istihlak voiding the sale; the Sahibayn give the buyer an option to rescind or share. Understand the reasoning and the practical difference.
This sub-issue also involves constructive possession via an order to mix, as in the examples of borrowing wheat to sow or giving a ring to a craftsman.
5
Apply to Modern Analogies
Consider how the principle translates: if a forward contract (salam) involves a fungible commodity, the obligation is in the liability; thus, the creditor's death or loss of the specific advance payment does not void the rescission. This logic also applies to currency exchange (sarf) where the subject matter is unspecified.
The text mentions sarf explicitly: rescission is valid after destruction of the substitutes because the obligation is unspecified.

إلى مسألة القرض قال في البدائع: وكذلك لو استقرض من رجل كراً ودفع إليه غرائره ليكيله فيها ففعل وهو غائب لم يكن قابضاً لأن القرض لا يملك قبل القبض فكان الكر على ملك المقرض فلم يصح الأمر ا هـ. قوله: (ولو قيمة الجارية في المسألتين. قبضها لأن شرط صحة الإقالة بقاء العقد وهو يبقى ببقاء المعقود عليه والمعقود عليه في السلم هو المسلم فيه وهو باق في ذمة المسلم إليه بعد هلاك الجارية، فإذا انفسخ العقد وجب عليه المبيعة لم تصح الإقالة وإذا تقايلا ثم ماتت بطلت الإقالة لأن المعقود عليه الجارية فلا بد من قيامها لصحة الإقالة وبقائها إلى أن تقبض. وقيد به لأن الإقالة في الصرف صحيحة بعد هلاك البدلين أو أحدهما باقية بعد الهلاك لأن المعقود عليه في الصرف ما وجب لكل واحد منهما في ذمة الآخر وهو غير معين فلا يتصور هلاكه قائماً. معيناً والمقبوض عين، ولذا لو كان المقبوض لم يتعين للرد بعد الإقالة. وفي القنية: تقايلا البيع في العبد فأبق من يد المشتري فإن لم يقدر على تسليمه بطلت الإقالة والبيع بحاله ا هـ. والحاصل أنه يشترط لصحة إقالة البيع قيام المبيع دون الثمن فلو تقايلا بعد هلاك الثمن ولو صحت ولكن لا بد من عدم الإبراء عنه لما في القنية: أبرأ البائع المشتري عن الثمن بعد قبض المبيع ثم تقابلا لا تصح ا.هـ. وقيد بهلاكها لأنها لو قطعت يدها ثم تقايلا صحت ولزمه رد جميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من أرش اليد إذا علم وقت الإقالة أنها قطعت يدها وأخذ المشتري أرشها وإن لم يعلم يخير المشتري بين الأخذ الثمن أو الترك؛ كذا بجميع القنية. ثم رقم الأشجار لا تسلم للمشتري وللبائع أن يأخذ قيمتها منه لأنها موجوده وقت البيع بخلاف تقايلا صحت الإقالة في الأرض بحصتها من الثمن، ولو اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار فقطعها ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة بجميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من قيمة الأشجار وتسلم الأشجار للمشتري. هذا إذا علم البائع بقطع الأشجار، وأما إذا لم يعلم به وقت الإقالة يخير إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك ا هـ.
And if the slave-girl was taken possession of and they rescinded and she died, or she died before the rescission, the [rescission] remains valid and is correct, and upon him is her value. And its opposite: her purchase from the orderer taking possession of the commodity and the debt. As for the commodity, the validity of possession is due to the validity of the order. As for the debt, it is due to its connection to his ownership because the commodity is deemed to be in his hand. And by the like of it one becomes a possessor, like one who borrows wheat and orders him to sow it in his land: the order is valid and the borrower becomes its possessor. And like one who gives a ring to a craftsman and orders him to add half a dinar from his own: it is valid and becomes a loan. In al-Idah: it does not mention whether if it perishes before delivery he becomes a possessor or not. He said: If we consider him a possessor, then the view is that mixing is consumption which is a cause of ownership. If he begins with the debt and then the commodity, he does not become a possessor. As for the debt, because the order is not valid regarding it. As for the commodity, because he mixed it with his own property before delivery such that it cannot be distinguished, so he becomes a consumer of the sale according to Abu Hanifa, so the sale is rescinded. This mixing is not approved by the buyer because his intention might be to begin with the commodity. According to them, the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he can rescind the sale; if he wishes, he can become a partner in the mixture because mixing is not consumption according to them. Thus in al-Hidaya, and Qadikhan restricted it to the view of Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, if he begins with the debt, he becomes a possessor of both, just as if he began with the commodity, due to necessity of connection to his ownership in both cases, since mixing is not consumption. Muhammad said: He becomes a possessor of the commodity but not of the debt, so they share in it and he is not free from the debt. He indicated by his saying 'in its container' that there is no food in it. If there was food in the container belonging to the owner of the salam before, he does not become a possessor, because his order is not valid regarding the property of another. In al-Mabsut: The most correct according to me is that he becomes a possessor because his order to mix the salam food with food in a way that it cannot be distinguished is valid, so he becomes a possessor by it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And the author indicated by the salam case the loan case. In al-Bada'i: Likewise, if one borrows a kur from a man and gives him his grain sacks to measure in them, and he does so while absent, he is not a possessor because a loan is not owned before possession, so the kur remains the property of the lender, so the order is not valid. End. Then he said: (If he gives a slave-girl as salam for a kur, and the slave-girl is received, and they rescind, and she dies, or she dies before the rescission, the rescission remains sound and correct, and he must pay her value.) That is, the rescission contract remains when they rescind while she is alive then she dies, and initiating the rescission contract is valid if they rescind after her death, and the salam seller must pay the value of the slave-girl in both cases. Because the condition for the validity of rescission is the continuation of the contract, and it continues with the continuation of the subject matter, and the subject matter in salam is the due goods, which remains in the liability of the salam seller after the death of the slave-girl. So when the contract is dissolved, he must return the slave-girl, and having failed due to her death, he must pay her value, just as if they had exchanged goods and then rescinded after the death of one of them, or one died after rescission. And possession was considered because it is the cause of liability, like usurpation. He said: (And its opposite: buying her for a thousand.) That is, if the sold slave-girl dies, the rescission is not valid. And if they rescind and then she dies, the rescission becomes void because the subject matter is the slave-girl, so she must exist for the validity of rescission and remain until she is taken. It is restricted because rescission in currency exchange (sarf) is valid after the death of either or both substitutes, remaining after death, because the subject matter in sarf is what each owes the other, which is unspecified, so it cannot be imagined to perish as a specific entity. And the received is a specific thing, so if the received had not been specified for return after rescission... In al-Qunyah: They rescinded the sale of a slave and he ran away from the buyer's hand; if he cannot deliver him, the rescission is void and the sale remains as it was. End. The summary: For the validity of rescission of a sale, the sold item must exist, but not the price. If they rescind after the destruction of the price, it is valid, but there must be no discharge from it. According to al-Qunyah: If the seller discharged the buyer from the price after possession of the sold item, then they rescinded, it is not valid. End. And he restricted it to her death because if her hand was cut off and then they rescinded, it is valid and he must return the entire price, and the seller has nothing for the diya of the hand if he knew at the time of rescission that her hand was cut off and the buyer took her diya. If he did not know, the buyer has the choice between taking the price or leaving it. Thus in all of al-Qunyah. Then the value of trees is not delivered to the buyer, and the seller may take their value from him because they existed at the time of sale, unlike the diya because it did not enter into the sale at all, neither intentionally nor implicitly. And before that: He bought land with crops and the crops ripened in his hand, then they rescinded: the rescission is not valid because the contract was only on the stalks, not on the wheat. If the buyer harvested the crops and then they rescinded, the rescission is valid for the land with its share of the price. If he bought land with trees and cut them, then they rescinded, the rescission is valid with the entire price, and the seller has nothing from the value of the trees, and the trees are delivered to the buyer. This is if the seller knew of the cutting of the trees. If he did not know at the time of rescission, he has the choice: if he wishes, he takes them with the entire price; if he wishes, he leaves them. End.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling (hukm) on the effect of a slave-girl's death in the context of an iqalah (mutual rescission) in a salam contract. The matn states that if the slave-girl, which was given as the capital in a salam sale, dies after being received by the seller and after an iqalah, the iqalah remains valid and the seller must pay her value. Conversely, if she dies before the iqalah, the iqalah is invalid. The same applies if she dies after iqalah but before return. The commentator explains the rationale: the condition for iqalah is the continued existence of the subject matter of the original contract. In salam, the subject matter is the debt (the musallam fih), which remains in the seller's liability even after the slave-girl's death. Therefore, the iqalah is not voided. The reverse case (buying back) is analogous: if the sold slave-girl dies before iqalah, the iqalah is invalid because the specific subject matter (the slave-girl) is gone. He also discusses the condition that only the sold item must exist for iqalah, not the price. Various sub-issues are explored: mixing of goods leading to istihlak (consumption) and the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on whether mixing counts as istihlak, the effect of the slave running away, the cutting of a hand, trees and land, etc. These illustrate the principle that iqalah is valid for the part that can be returned.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract; both parties agree to annul the sale.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance and the goods are delivered at a future date; capital is the advance payment.
قبض (qabḍ): Taking possession; physical or constructive delivery of the subject matter.
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing of goods belonging to different parties such that they become indistinguishable.
المسلم فيه (al-musallam fīh): The goods to be delivered in a salam contract; the subject matter of the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a valid iqalah in a salam contract after the capital (paid in advance) has perished?
A) The capital must be returned in kind.
B) The subject matter of the salam (the debt) must still exist.
C) Both the capital and the debt must exist.
D) The buyer must agree to forgive the capital.

Key Takeaway
The validity of iqalah depends on the continued existence of the contract's subject matter: in salam, that is the debt in the liability, not the specific capital.

Dispute Over Description and Term in Salam: Who Is Believed
Ruling on whose claim is accepted when disputing description or term in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on who is believed when disputing a description or term in a salam contract and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In a salam dispute, the claimant of the stipulation is believed because he claims validity, not the denier.

When a salam contract is disputed over whether a description or term was stipulated, the normal rule that the defendant's statement holds is reversed. This is because salam requires both a specific description and a fixed term for validity. The claimant of the stipulation is asserting that the contract was validly concluded, and the default is that Muslims engage in valid transactions. In this lesson, we examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement on this point and the concept of obstinacy (muta'annit).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
In a salam contract, two parties differ: one claims a description (e.g., defective) or a term was stipulated; the other denies it. The question is: whose statement is accepted?
2
Recognize the general Hanafi principle
The default rule in disputes is that the denier (munkir) is believed (al-bayyina ʿalā al-muddaʿī wa al-yamīn ʿalā al-munkir). However, in salam, the necessity of description and term creates an exception.
The text states: 'He is the claimant of validity, since salam is only valid with a term and a description.'
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the claimant of the stipulation is always believed, regardless of obstinacy. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the denier is believed unless he is muta'annit (obstinate), i.e., denies something that benefits him.
Obstinate means one who denies what benefits him.
4
Apply the ruling to specific scenarios
The passage covers cases where the claimant is the owner of the salam (rabb al-salam) or the seller (muslam ilayh), and whether the dispute is over description or term. The rule applies uniformly.
The text also notes that if the owner's statement is accepted regarding the term, it also applies to the amount of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والقول لمدعي الرداءة والتأجيل لا لنافي الوصف والأجل) أي إذا اختلفا في اشتراط وصف السلم بأن قال أحدهما شرطناه رديئاً وقال الآخر لم نشترط شيئاً أو قال أحدهما شرطنا الأجل وقال الآخر لم نشترط شيئاً كان القول لمن ادعى الاشتراط فيهما لا لمن نفاه فيهما لأنه مدعي الصحة إذ السلم لا يجوز إلا مؤجلاً موصوفاً فشهد له الظاهر لأن الفاسد حرام والظاهر أن المسلم لا يباشره أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان رب السلم مدعي الوصف أو المسلم إليه وفي الأول خلافهما، فالإمام علل بأنه مدعي الصحة وهما عللا بأن المسلم إليه منكر فالقول له. وشمل أيضاً ما إذا كان مدعي الأجل المسلم إليه أو رب السلم وفي الأول خلافهما لإنكاره وإذا قبل في الثاني قول رب السلم اتفاقاً رجع إليه في مقدار الأجل أيضاً فيقبل قوله في أصله ومقداره. والأصل عند الإمام أن القول لمدعي الصحة سواء كان الآخر متعنتاً أو لا، وعندهما القول للمنكر إن لم يكن متعنتاً وهو من أنكر ما ينفعه، وغير المتعنت من أنكر ما يضر. هذا في الشريعة وأما المتعنت في اللغة فهو من يطلب العنت وهو الوقوع فيما لا يستطيع الإنسان الخروج عنه؛ كذا في البناية. ولو قال المصنف «والقول لمدعي الوصف الخ» قال في النهر: هذا أي قول المصنف والقول لمدعي الرداءة صادق بما إذا قال أحدهما شرطنا رديئاً فقال الآخر لم نشرط شيئاً وبما إذا ادعى
He said: (And the statement belongs to the one who claims defectiveness and term, not to the one who denies the description and the term.) That is, if they differ regarding the stipulation of the description of the salam: one says 'We stipulated it defective' and the other says 'We stipulated nothing', or one says 'We stipulated the term' and the other says 'We stipulated nothing', then the statement is of the one who claims the stipulation in both, not of the one who denies it, because he is the one claiming validity, since salam is only valid with a term and a description, so the apparent circumstance supports him because the corrupt is forbidden and the apparent circumstance is that a Muslim would not engage in it. It is general and includes whether the owner of the salam is the claimant of the description or the salam seller. Regarding the first, there is disagreement between them: Imam [Abu Hanifa] reasoned that he is the claimant of validity, while they [the two] reasoned that the salam seller is the denier, so the statement is his. It also includes whether the claimant of the term is the salam seller or the owner of the salam. Regarding the first, there is disagreement between them due to his denial. If the statement of the owner of the salam is accepted in the second by agreement, then [the decision] returns to him also regarding the amount of the term, so his statement is accepted in its principle and its amount. The principle according to Imam is that the statement belongs to the claimant of validity whether the other is obstinate or not. According to them, the statement belongs to the denier if he is not obstinate. The obstinate is one who denies what benefits him, and the non-obstinate is one who denies what harms him. This is in the Sacred Law. As for the obstinate in language, he is one who seeks hardship, i.e., to fall into something from which a person cannot escape. Thus in al-Binaya. If the author said 'And the statement belongs to the claimant of the description, etc.', he said in al-Nahr: This, i.e., the statement of the author, 'and the statement belongs to the claimant of defectiveness' is true when one says 'We stipulated it defective' and the other says 'We stipulated nothing', and when he claims...
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a dispute in a salam contract concerning whether a defect (radi'a) or a term (ajal) was stipulated. The matn states that the one who claims the stipulation is believed, not the one who denies it. The commentator explains the reasoning: because salam is only valid with a specified description and a fixed term, the claim of stipulation is a claim of validity (sihha). By default, Muslims engage in valid contracts, so the apparent circumstance supports the claimant. The text then details an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the claimant of the stipulation is always believed based on the principle 'al-qawl li-mudda'i al-sihha' (the statement is for the claimant of validity) regardless of obstinacy. The two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the denier (al-munkir) is generally believed unless he is obstinate (muta'annit). The concept of muta'annit is defined: one who denies what benefits him (i.e., denies a stipulation that would benefit him). The passage also gives the linguistic definition from al-Binaya. The overall rule is applied to both the description and the term, with specific cases for who claims what.

Key Terms
الرداءة (al-radā'ah): Defectiveness or poor quality; in salam, a description of the goods being of low quality (if stipulated).
التأجيل (al-ta'jīl): The stipulation of a fixed term for delivery in salam.
متعنت (mutaʿannit): Obstinate; a party who denies something that would benefit them.
مدعي الصحة (muddaʿī al-ṣiḥḥa): Claimant of validity; the one who asserts that the contract was correctly formed with all required conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over whether a salam contract had a stipulated term (ajal), who is believed according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The one who denies the term is believed, as he is the denier.
B) The one who claims the term is believed, because he is claiming validity.
C) The one who is obstinate is believed regardless of claim.
D) Both parties are equally believed and the contract is void.

Key Takeaway
In salam disputes, the claimant of a stipulation is believed because salam requires description and term, making the claimant a claimant of validity.

Shurut Sihhat al-Iqala: The Sold Item Must Exist
Explains conditions for rescission (iqala) when the object or price has been destroyed or changed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid iqala and identify when iqala is batil due to destruction of the sold item or prior ibra'.

Core Idea
Iqala is a new sale requiring the sold item to exist at the time of rescission.

In Hanafi fiqh, iqala is treated as a new contract, not mere cancellation. Therefore, like any sale, it requires the subject matter (the sold item) to exist. This lesson examines the key conditions from the text, including exceptions when the price perishes, the effect of prior release (ibra'), and cases where the item is destroyed or damaged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
Read the passage's central principle: 'يشترط لصحة إقالة البيع قيام المبيع دون الثمن'. This means the sold item (مبيع) must exist, not the price.
The price can be destroyed; iqala remains valid.
2
Understand the consequence of destruction of the sold item
If the sold item is destroyed completely (e.g., slave absconds and cannot be delivered), iqala is invalid and the original sale remains.
Inability to deliver the item voids iqala.
3
Analyze the effect of prior ibra' (release of price)
If the seller releases the buyer from the price (ibra'), then they perform iqala, the iqala is invalid. Ibra' must not have occurred.
Ibra' is a preventer (mani') for iqala.
4
Examine cases of partial damage (e.g., loss of hand)
If the sold item is damaged but still exists, iqala is valid, but the seller may not claim compensation for the damage if known; otherwise the buyer has a choice.
Knowledge of damage at time of iqala affects the ruling.
5
Apply to trees and crops
The text distinguishes between existing items (trees) and harvested crops. If crops are harvested, iqala only applies to land with its share of price. For cut trees, iqala valid with full price, trees go to buyer.
This illustrates the 'existence' condition in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الصرف ما وجب لكل واحد منهما في ذمة الآخر وهو غير معين فلا يتصور هلاكه قائماً. معيناً والمقبوض عين، ولذا لو كان المقبوض لم يتعين للرد بعد الإقالة. وفي القنية : تقايلا البيع في العبد فأبق من يد المشتري فإن لم يقدر على تسليمه بطلت الإقالة والبيع بحاله ا هـ . والحاصل أنه يشترط لصحة إقالة البيع قيام المبيع دون الثمن فلو تقايلا بعد هلاك الثمن ولو صحت ولكن لا بد من عدم الإبراء عنه لما في القنية : أبرأ البائع المشتري عن الثمن بعد قبض المبيع ثم تقابلا لا تصح ا.هـ. وقيد بهلاكها لأنها لو قطعت يدها ثم تقايلا صحت ولزمه رد جميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من أرش اليد إذا علم وقت الإقالة أنها قطعت يدها وأخذ المشتري أرشها وإن لم يعلم يخير المشتري بين الأخذ الثمن أو الترك؛ كذا بجميع القنية . ثم رقم الأشجار لا تسلم للمشتري وللبائع أن يأخذ قيمتها منه لأنها موجوده وقت البيع بخلاف الأرش لأنه لم يدخل في البيع أصلاً لا قصداً ولا ضمناً. وقال قبله : اشترى أرضاً مع الزرع وأدرك الزرع في يده ثم تقايلا لا تجوز الإقالة لأن العقد إنما ورد على القصيل دون الحنطة، ولو حصد المشتري الزرع ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة في الأرض بحصتها من الثمن، ولو اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار فقطعها ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة بجميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من قيمة الأشجار وتسلم الأشجار للمشتري . هذا إذا علم البائع بقطع الأشجار، وأما إذا لم يعلم به وقت الإقالة يخير إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك ا هـ .
In currency exchange, what is owed by each to the other in the dhimmah is unspecified and thus its destruction as an existing entity is inconceivable. The received item is a specific entity, so if the received item had not become specified, it cannot be returned after iqala. In al-Qunyah: They rescind a sale of a slave and the slave absconds from the buyer's hand; if he cannot deliver it, the iqala is invalid and the sale remains as it was. The summary is that for the validity of iqala of a sale, the sold item must exist, not the price. So if they rescind after the price has perished, it is valid, but it is necessary that there was no release (ibra') from it, as in al-Qunyah: The seller releases the buyer from the price after taking possession of the sold item, then they rescind; it is not valid. And it is conditioned by its destruction because if it lost its hand then they rescind, it is valid and he must return the entire price and nothing is owed to the seller for the compensation of the hand if he knew at the time of iqala that it had lost its hand and the buyer had taken its compensation. If he did not know, the buyer is given a choice between taking the price or leaving it. Similarly in all of al-Qunyah. Then the value of trees is not delivered to the buyer, and the seller may take their value from him because they existed at the time of sale, unlike the compensation because it did not enter the sale at all, neither intentionally nor implicitly. And before that he said: He bought land with crops and the crops ripened in his hand, then they rescind; iqala is not permissible because the contract was only made on the stalks, not the wheat. If the buyer harvested the crops then they rescind, iqala is valid for the land with its portion of the price. If he bought land with trees and cut them, then they rescind, iqala is valid with the entire price, and nothing is owed to the seller for the value of the trees, and the trees are delivered to the buyer. This is if the seller knew about the cutting of the trees. If he did not know at the time of iqala, he is given a choice: if he wishes, he takes it with the entire price, and if he wishes, he leaves it.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the conditions for the validity of iqala (rescission) in a sale, particularly when the sold item or the price has been altered or destroyed. The author begins with a principle from currency exchange (sarf): since what is owed is a debt in the dhimmah, it is not a specific item, so it cannot be destroyed as a specific entity. However, in regular sale, the received item is a specific entity; thus, if it had not become specified, it cannot be returned after iqala. He then cites al-Qunyah with examples: if the parties rescind a sale of a slave and the slave absconds, and the buyer cannot deliver it, the iqala is void and the original sale remains. The key condition is that the sold item must still exist at the time of iqala; the price need not exist. If the price perishes, iqala is still valid, provided there was no prior release (ibra') of the price. He then distinguishes between physical damage (like losing a hand) and complete destruction. If a slave loses a hand and the parties rescind, iqala is valid, and the buyer must return the full price; the seller gets no compensation for the lost hand if he knew about it. If he did not know, the buyer has the option to either take the price or leave the iqala. Next, he discusses trees and crops: if a buyer cuts trees on land before iqala, the seller may take their value because trees existed at the time of sale. In contrast, compensation for damage (arsh) does not enter the sale. If crops are harvested, iqala is valid only for the land with its share of the price. The ruling emphasizes that iqala is a new contract that requires the subject matter to exist for its validity.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract by the parties, returning the sold item and price.
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for a defect or injury causing decrease in value of a sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for the validity of iqala according to this passage?
A) Both the sold item and the price must exist.
B) The sold item must exist, but the price may have perished.
C) The price must exist, but the sold item may have perished.
D) Neither the sold item nor the price need exist.

Key Takeaway
The sold item must exist at the time of iqala; if it is destroyed, iqala is invalid and the original sale remains.

The Credibility Rule in Salam Disputes: Imam vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on credibility in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on credibility in salam disputes and apply the rule to determine who is believed in a given scenario.

Core Idea
The claimant of a stipulation is believed as he asserts validity, but the Sahibayn qualify this with the muta'annit exception.

When a salam contract is disputed regarding the stipulation of quality or term, a crucial question arises: who is to be believed? This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. We will explore their respective proofs and see how the principle of 'claiming validity' versus 'denying a claim' plays out in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the exact legal question: when parties in a salam contract dispute whether a description (quality) or term was stipulated, which party is believed?
The matn states the rule: the claimant of poor quality and term is believed.
2
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Imam holds that the claimant of the condition (quality or term) is believed because he claims the contract's validity. His dalil: salam is only valid with a term and description, so the claimant asserts a valid contract, and the apparent circumstance (zahir) supports him as a Muslim would not engage in an invalid contract.
Imam's view: القول لمدعي الصحة.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and dalil
The two companions hold that the denier (who denies the stipulation) is believed, because he is the one denying a claim. They add the condition that the denier is not muta'annit (one who denies what benefits him). Their dalil: the default is that the denier is believed, and they differentiate between muta'annit (denies benefit) and non-muta'annit (denies harm).
Sahibayn's view: القول للمنكر ما لم يكن متعنتاً.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and thamarah al-khilaf
Imam's response is that the claimant of the condition is the real claimant of validity; the denier is claiming invalidity, which is not default. The practical difference (thamarah): if the dispute is about poor quality, Imam believes the seller (who claims poor quality was stipulated), while the Sahibayn believe the buyer (who denies it), unless the buyer is muta'annit.
The text does not give explicit tarjih, but later practice often favors Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والقول لمدعي الرداءة والتأجيل لا لنافي الوصف والأجل) أي إذا اختلفا في اشتراط وصف السلم بأن قال أحدهما شرطناه رديئاً وقال الآخر لم نشترط شيئاً أو قال أحدهما شرطنا الأجل وقال الآخر لم نشترط شيئاً كان القول لمن ادعى الاشتراط فيهما لا لمن نفاه فيهما لأنه مدعي الصحة إذ السلم لا يجوز إلا مؤجلاً موصوفاً فشهد له الظاهر لأن الفاسد حرام والظاهر أن المسلم لا يباشره أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان رب السلم مدعي الوصف أو المسلم إليه وفي الأول خلافهما، فالإمام علل بأنه مدعي الصحة وهما عللا بأن المسلم إليه منكر فالقول له. وشمل أيضاً ما إذا كان مدعي الأجل المسلم إليه أو رب السلم وفي الأول خلافهما لإنكاره وإذا قبل في الثاني قول رب السلم اتفاقاً رجع إليه في مقدار الأجل أيضاً فيقبل قوله في أصله ومقداره. والأصل عند الإمام أن القول لمدعي الصحة سواء كان الآخر متعنتاً أو لا، وعندهما القول للمنكر إن لم يكن متعنتاً وهو من أنكر ما ينفعه، وغير المتعنت من أنكر ما يضر. هذا في الشريعة وأما المتعنت في اللغة فهو من يطلب العنت وهو الوقوع فيما لا يستطيع الإنسان الخروج عنه ؛ كذا في البناية. ولو قال المصنف «والقول لمدعي قوله : (ولو قال المصنف والقول لمدعي الوصف الخ قال في النهر : هذا أي قول المصنف والقول لمدعي الرداءة صادق بما إذا قال أحدهما شرطنا رديئاً فقال الآخر لم نشرط شيئاً وبما إذا ادعى أن الآخر اشتراط الجودة وقال الآخر إنما شرطنا ردية والمراد الأول ولذا أردفه بقوله لا لنا في الوصف والأجل، ولإفادة أن الرداءة مثال حتى لو قال أحدهما شرطنا جيداً وقال الآخر لم نشرط شيئاً فالحكم أن الوصف الشامل للرداءة والجودة لكان أولى لأن أحدهما لو قال شرطناه جيداً ونفى الآخر الاشتراط أصلاً فالقول للمثبت قيد الاختلاف في أصل التأجيل لأنهما لو اختلفا في مقداره فالقول للطالب اليمين لإنكاره الزيادة وأي برهن قبل وإن برهنا قضى ببينة المطلوب لإثباتها الزيادة. وإن اختلفا في مضيه فالقول للمطلوب لإنكاره توجه المطالبة، فإن برهنا قضى ببينة المطلوب لإثباتها زيادة الأجل فالقول قوله أي المسلم إليه والبينة بينته، أما إذا نظرنا إلى الصورة فهو منكر، وإن نظرنا إلى المعنى فمعناه ثبوت الحق في الشهر المستقبل فإذا أقاما البينة فبينة المسلم إليه بمعناها أثبتنا حقاً له في شهر لم يتعرض ببينة رب السلم لذلك الشهر فكانت بينته أولى؛ كذا في إيضاح الكرماني. ثم اعلم أن بين الأجل والوصف فرقاً وهو الاختلاف في مقدار الأجل يعني أنه ما هو لا يوجب التحالف وفي الوصف يوجبه لكونه يجري مجرى الأصل. وفي الخلاصة : إذا شرط في السلم الثوب الجيد فجاء بثوب وادعى أنه جيد وأنكر الطالب فالقاضي يرى اثنين من أهل تلك الصنعة وهذا أحوط والواحد يكفي، فإن قالا جيد أجبره على القبول فإذا اختلفا في السلم يتحالفان استحساناً ويبدأ بيمين المطلوب عند أبي يوسف ثم رجع وقال بيمين الطالب وهو قول محمد، وأي برهن قبل، فإن برهنا قضى ببينة رب السلم بسلم واحد عند أبي يوسف ويقال هو قول أبي حنيفة والمسألة على ثلاثة أوجه : لأن رأس المال إما عين أو دين وكل وجه على ثلاثة أوجه : اتفقا على رأس المال واختلفا في المسلم فيه أو على القلب أو اختلفا فيهما. فإن كان رأس المال عيناً واختلفا في المسلم فيه لا غير فقال الطالب هذا الثوب في كر حنطة وقال الآخر في نصف كر أو في شعير أو في الحنطة الرديئة وأقاما البينة قضى ببينة رب السلم اجماعاً، وإن اختلفا في رأس المال فقال أحدهما هذا الثوب وقال الآخر هذا العبد واتفقا في المسلم فيه أنه الحنطة أو قال أحدهما هذا الثوب في كر حنطة وقال الآخر في كر شعير وأقاما البينة قضى بالمسلمين، فمحمد رحمه الله على أصله، وأبو يوسف يقول كل يدعي عقداً غير ما يدعيه الآخر. وإن كان رأس المال دراهم أو دنانير إن اتفقا في رأس المال واختلفا في المسلم فيه وأقاما البينة فالبينة لرب السلم ويقضي بسلم واحد عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد، وإن كان الاختلاف على القلب فعلى هذا الاختلاف ولو اختلفا فيهما فقال أحدهما عشرة دراهم في كري حنطة وقال الآخر خمسة عشر في كر وأقاما البينة فعند أبي يوسف تثبت الزيادة فيجب خمسة عشر في كرين ولا يقضي بسلمين، وعند محمد يقضي بسلمين. عقد بخمسة عشر في كر وعقد بعشرة في كرين ولو ادعى أحدهما أن رأس المال دراهم والآخر دنانير لم يذكر هذا وينبغي أن الآخر اشتراط الجودة وقال الآخر إنما شرطنا ردية والمراد الأول ولذا أردفه بقوله لا لنا في الوصف والأجل، ولإفادة أن الرداءة مثال حتى لو قال أحدهما شرطنا جيداً وقال الآخر لم نشرط شيئاً فالحكم
His statement: (And the saying is for the claimant of poor quality and term, not for the denier of description and term.) That is, if they differ regarding the stipulation of a description in salam: one says 'we stipulated it as poor quality' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything', or one says 'we stipulated the term' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything', then the saying is for the one who claims the stipulation in both cases, not for the one who denies it, because he is the claimant of validity, since salam is only valid with a term and a description. So the apparent circumstance supports him because the invalid is forbidden and the apparent is that a Muslim does not engage in it. He made it unrestricted, so it includes whether the rabb al-salam is the claimant of description or the muslam ilayh. In the first there is disagreement between them (the two companions): Imam (Abu Hanifa) reasoned that he is the claimant of validity, while they reasoned that the muslam ilayh is the denier, so the saying is for him. It also includes whether the claimant of term is the muslam ilayh or the rabb al-salam. In the first there is disagreement between them due to his denial. And if the saying of the rabb al-salam is accepted by agreement in the second, he is also referred back to regarding the amount of the term; so his saying is accepted in its origin and its amount. The principle according to Imam is that the saying is for the claimant of validity, whether the other is muta'annit or not. According to them (the two companions), the saying is for the denier if he is not muta'annit, and muta'annit is one who denies what benefits him, and non-muta'annit is one who denies what harms him. This is in sharee'ah. As for muta'annit in language, it is one who seeks 'anat (difficulty), which is falling into something from which one cannot escape. Similarly in al-Binayah. And if the author had said 'And the saying is for the claimant of description...' His statement: (And if the author had said 'And the saying is for the claimant of description etc.' In al-Nahr: This, i.e., the author's saying 'And the saying is for the claimant of poor quality' applies when one says 'we stipulated poor quality' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything', and also when he claims that the other stipulated good quality and the other says 'we only stipulated poor quality'. The first is intended, so he appended with his statement 'not for the denier of description and term', to indicate that poor quality is an example. So even if one says 'we stipulated good quality' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything', the ruling is that the description includes both poor and good quality; it would have been better because if one says 'we stipulated good quality' and the other denies the stipulation entirely, then the saying is for the affirmer. [This is] restricted to disagreement on the origin of the term, because if they differ on its amount, then the saying is for the seeker of the oath due to his denial of the increase. And any evidence is accepted. If they both present evidence, the evidence of the defendant is judged to prove the increase. If they differ on its passing, then the saying is for the defendant due to his denial of the claim. If they both present evidence, the evidence of the defendant is judged to prove the increase of the term. So the saying is his, i.e., the muslam ilayh, and his evidence is his evidence. If we look at the form, he is a denier; if we look at the meaning, it means the establishment of a right in the future month. So if they both present evidence, the evidence of the muslam ilayh in its meaning establishes a right for him in a month that the evidence of the rabb al-salam did not address that month, so his evidence is more worthy. Similarly in Idah al-Kirmani. Then know that there is a difference between term and description: disagreement in the amount of the term does not necessitate mutual oath-taking (tahaluf), while in description it does, because it is like the origin. In al-Khulasa: If good quality is stipulated in salam and he brings a cloth and claims it is good, and the claimant denies, the judge appoints two experts from that craft, and one is sufficient as a precaution. If they say it is good, he compels the claimant to accept. If they differ in salam, they take oaths by istihsan, beginning with the defendant's oath according to Abu Yusuf, then he retracted and said the claimant's oath, which is the saying of Muhammad. Any evidence is accepted. If they both present evidence, the evidence of the rabb al-salam is judged for one salam according to Abu Yusuf, and it is said that this is the saying of Abu Hanifa. The issue has three aspects: because the capital is either a specific item or a debt, and each aspect has three aspects: they agree on the capital and differ on the muslam fih (the subject of salam), or on the reversal (qalb), or differ on both. If the capital is a specific item and they differ only on the muslam fih: the claimant says 'this cloth in exchange for a kurr of wheat' and the other says 'for half a kurr' or 'for barley' or 'for poor quality wheat', and they present evidence, the evidence of the rabb al-salam is judged by consensus. If they differ on the capital: one says 'this cloth' and the other says 'this slave' and they agree on the muslam fih that it is wheat, or one says 'this cloth in exchange for a kurr of wheat' and the other says 'in exchange for a kurr of barley', and they present evidence, two salams are judged. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) is on his principle, and Abu Yusuf says each claims a contract different from what the other claims. If the capital is dirhams or dinars, if they agree on the capital and differ on the muslam fih and present evidence, the evidence is for the rabb al-salam and one salam is judged according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. If the disagreement is on the reversal, then there is disagreement. And if they differ on both: one says 'ten dirhams in exchange for a kurr of wheat' and the other says 'fifteen in exchange for a kurr', and they present evidence, according to Abu Yusuf the increase is established, so fifteen is owed in two kurrs, and two salams are not judged. According to Muhammad, two salams are judged: a contract for fifteen in a kurr and a contract for ten in two kurrs. If one claims the capital is dirhams and the other dinars, this is not mentioned. And it should be that the other stipulated good quality, and the other says 'we only stipulated poor quality', and the first is intended, so he appended with his statement 'not for the denier of description and term', to indicate that poor quality is an example, so even if one says 'we stipulated good quality' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything', the ruling...
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about who is believed when the parties to a salam contract disagree on whether a description (quality) or a term (ajal) was stipulated. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states: 'والقول لمدعي الرداءة والتأجيل لا لنافي الوصف والأجل' — the saying (i.e., the credible party) is the one who claims poor quality and a term, not the one who denies the description and term. The commentary explains that if one party says 'we stipulated poor quality' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything', or similarly for term, the claimant of the stipulation is believed because he is claiming the validity of the contract; salam is only valid with a term and description. The apparent circumstance (zahir) supports him because the invalid is forbidden and a Muslim would not engage in it. The commentary then details a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the claimant of the condition is the one who asserts validity (mudda'i al-sihhah), so his word is taken. The two companions hold that the denier (the one who denies the stipulation) is the one who is believed, because he is the one denying something against him, and the principle is that the denier's word is taken unless he is muta'annit (one who denies what benefits him). The text then differentiates between disagreement on the origin of the term and its amount: for the origin, the claimant is believed; for the amount, the one who seeks the oath (the claimant) is believed, but if both present evidence, the defendant's evidence is preferred. Further, there is a difference between description and term: disagreement on the amount of term does not lead to mutual oaths (tahaluf), while disagreement on description does, because description is like the origin. The passage also discusses various scenarios where the capital is a specific item or debt, and how evidence is weighed. The later part from al-Khulasa explains that if a specific quality (e.g., good) is stipulated and the seller brings a cloth claiming it matches, the judge appoints experts. If they differ, they take oaths by istihsan. The passage concludes with detailed rules on evidence when the parties disagree on capital and subject matter, highlighting differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
متعنت (mutaʿannit): One who denies something that benefits him, thus his denial is not credible.
تحالف (taḥāluf): Mutual oath-taking between parties when both lack evidence; each swears that the other is lying.
القول لمدعي الصحة (al-qawl li-muddaʿī al-ṣiḥḥa): The saying is for the claimant of validity – the party asserting the contract's validity is believed.
القول للمنكر (al-qawl li-l-munkir): The saying is for the denier – the party denying a claim is believed by default.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract dispute about the term, the buyer says 'we stipulated a 2-month term' and the seller says 'we stipulated no term'. According to the Sahibayn, who is believed if the seller is not muta'annit?
A) The buyer, because he is the claimant of the condition.
B) The seller, because he is the denier and not muta'annit.
C) The buyer, because he is the denier.
D) Neither; they must take mutual oaths.

Key Takeaway
In salam disputes over stipulations, the credibility rule differs between Imam Abu Hanifa (claimant believed) and the Sahibayn (denier believed unless muta'annit), and the practical outcome depends on whether the denier is denying something beneficial or harmful.

Disputes in Salam: Evidence and Oaths for Description and Term
General rules and oath ikhtilaf for salam disputes over description and term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for who is believed in disputes over stipulation, amount, and passing of the term and description in a salam contract, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on the order of oaths.

Core Idea
In salam, the affirmant is believed on stipulations; the claimant on term amount; the defendant on term passing; and description disputes require mutual oaths.

Salam contracts often lead to disputes about the quality (wasf) and delivery date (ajal). The Hanafi school has clear procedural rules: the party affirming a stipulation is believed; for the amount of the term, the claimant (rabb al-salam) prevails with an oath; for whether the term has passed, the defendant (muslim ilayh) is believed. A key distinction is that disputes over the amount of the term do not lead to mutual oaths (tahaluf), while disputes over description do. An important intra-Hanafi difference exists on who takes the oath first: Abu Yusuf initially said the defendant, then retracted to the claimant, aligning with Muhammad's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute type
Classify the disagreement: is it over the existence of a stipulation (e.g., 'good quality'), the amount of the term, or whether the term has passed?
Each type has a different rule for whose word is accepted.
2
Apply the rule for the dispute type
For a stipulation, the affirmant (muthbit) is believed. For the amount of term, the claimant (rabb al-salam) is believed with an oath. For the passing of term, the defendant (muslim ilayh) is believed.
If both present proof, the defendant's evidence is preferred when it establishes an increase.
3
Distinguish between term amount and description
Dispute over the amount of term does not require mutual oaths (tahaluf), but dispute over description does, because description is considered like the original subject matter.
This is a crucial procedural distinction.
4
Trace the oath order ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf initially held that the oath begins with the defendant, then retracted and adopted the claimant's oath first, which is Muhammad's position. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is the latter.
The retraction is based on reconsideration of the principle.
5
Apply when evidence is presented
If both parties bring proof in a description dispute, the judge rules with the evidence of the rabb al-salam for one contract (according to Abu Yusuf). This connects to the next lesson.
This is a bridge to the detailed classification.

If the capital is a specific item and they dispute over the subject of delivery only, and the claimant says 'this garment for a load of wheat' while the other says 'for half a load or for barley or for poor-quality wheat,' and they present evidence, judgment is given with the evidence of the rabb al-salam by consensus. If the dispute is over the counter-value, then it follows this difference. If they dispute over both: one says 'ten dirhams for two loads of wheat' and the other says 'fifteen for one load' and present evidence, then according to Abu Yusuf the increase is established, so fifteen for two loads is obligatory, and judgment is not given for two salam contracts, while according to Muhammad judgment is given for two salam contracts: a contract for fifteen for one load and a contract for ten for two loads. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir. The conclusion is that if they dispute over genus, description, or amount, they take mutual oaths, whether regarding the capital or the subject of delivery. If they dispute over the stipulation of description or term, then the word is for the affirmant, not the denier. If they dispute over the amount of the term, then the word is for the rabb al-salam. If they dispute over its passing, then the word is for the Muslim ilayh. If they dispute over specifying the place of fulfillment, then the word is for the defendant, and if over its stipulation, then for the one who affirms it. In al-Zahiriyya: If they dispute over the genus of the subject of contract, they take mutual oaths, and likewise over description, unlike the dispute over description in the sale of a specific item. If they dispute over the place of fulfillment, the word is for the defendant, and if they present proof, then for the claimant according to him, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they take mutual oaths and return the salam. It is said the opposite. In al-Sihah: rada'a means decay, so radi' means corrupt, and arda'tuhu means I corrupted it. End. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned first in the claim 'ta'jil' and in the denial 'ajal,' so apparently there is no difference between them according to him, but it is not so because in al-Qamus: ajal is the ultimate time for death, payment of debt, and the duration of something, plural ajali, and ta'jil is specifying ajal. End. Specifying means determining, and we stated earlier that if they dispute over its amount, the word is for the claimant, so it is determined that ta'jil in his speech is used metaphorically as ajal. End. By the second evidence.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of disputes that may arise in a salam contract, focusing on disputes over description (wasf) and term (ajal). The author explains that when one party affirms a stipulation and the other denies, the burden of proof is on the affirmant. For disputes over the duration of the term, the claimant (rabb al-salam) is believed with an oath. If both present evidence, the defendant's evidence is preferred because they establish an increase. The text then distinguishes between disputes over the amount of the term (which do not lead to mutual oaths) and disputes over description (which do). Intra-Hanafi differences are noted: Abu Yusuf initially said the oath begins with the defendant, then retracted to the claimant's oath, which is also Muhammad's view. The author also discusses cases where the capital is a specific item versus debt, with Imam Muhammad asserting two separate contracts when both claim different contracts, while Abu Yusuf often rules for one contract with the increase established. The passage concludes with a linguistic clarification: 'ta'jil' (specifying a term) is often used metaphorically as 'ajal' (the term itself) in the context of the matn, based on the ruling that the claimant's word is accepted for the amount.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale: paying for goods to be delivered later, with full payment now.
أجل (ajal): The fixed term or deadline for delivery in a salam contract.
تأجيل (ta'jil): The act of specifying or setting a term for delivery.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oaths taken by both parties when they disagree and evidence is insufficient.

Key Takeaway
In salam disputes, the affirmant is believed on stipulations, the claimant on term amount, the defendant on term passage; description triggers mutual oaths, and the claimant begins the oath per the mufta bihi.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on One vs. Two Contracts in Salam
Detailed classification of disputes leading to one or two salam contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for when a dispute over capital and subject matter in salam leads to one contract or two, distinguishing the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
When both parties present evidence, Abu Yusuf rules for one salam contract with increased capital; Muhammad rules for two separate contracts.

When both parties in a salam contract bring evidence, the judge must decide whether there is one contract or two. The Hanafi school has a detailed classification based on whether the capital is a specific item or debt, and whether the dispute is over the subject matter (muslim fih) or the capital itself. Abu Yusuf (aligned with Abu Hanifa) generally rules for one contract with the increase established by the plaintiff's evidence, while Imam Muhammad holds that each party's evidence establishes a separate contract. This lesson unpacks the three aspects of the issue and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the capital type
Classify the capital (ra's al-mal) as either a specific item ('ayn) or a debt (dayn, e.g., dirhams/dinars).
The rules differ based on this.
2
Determine the dispute focus
Is the dispute over the subject matter (muslim fih), the capital, or both? Each subcase has a specific ruling.
Use the three-aspect framework: agree on capital/dispute on subject, or vice versa, or both disputed.
3
Apply the rule for specific item capital
If capital is a specific item and only the subject matter is disputed, judgment is for one salam by consensus. If the capital itself is disputed (e.g., garment vs. slave), Muhammad rules for two contracts; Abu Yusuf says each claims a different contract.
This is the core of the ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the rule for debt capital
If capital is dirhams/dinars and they agree on the capital but dispute the subject, Abu Yusuf rules one contract; Muhammad argues that the dispute over the subject with same capital also leads to two contracts according to his principle.
Abu Yusuf's view is the mufta bihi in this case.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, one contract is enforced with increased obligation; under Muhammad, two separate contracts are enforced, each with its own terms.
This affects the obligations of both parties.

أن الوصف الشامل للرداءة والجودة لكان أولى لأن أحدهما لو قال شرطناه جيداً ونفى الآخر الاشتراط أصلاً فالقول للمثبت قيد الاختلاف في أصل التأجيل لأنهما لو اختلفا في مقداره فالقول للطالب اليمين لإنكاره والآخر دنانير لم يذكر هذا وينبغي أن الآخر اشتراط الجودة وقال الآخر إنما شرطنا ردية والمراد الأول ولذا أردفه بقوله لا لنا في الوصف والأجل، ولإفادة أن الرداءة مثال حتى لو قال أحدهما شرطنا جيداً وقال الآخر لم نشرط شيئاً فالحكم يقضي بسلمين كما في الثوبين؛ كذا في فتح القدير والحاصل أنهما إن اختلفا في الجنس والصفة أو المقدار تحالفا، سواء كان في رأس المال أو في المسلم إليه. وإن اختلفا في اشتراط الوصف أو الأجل فالقول لمثبته لا النافيه، وإن اختلفا في مقدار الأجل فالقول لرب السلم، وإن اختلفا في مضيه فالقول للمسلم إليه، وإن اختلفا في بيان مكان الإيفاء فالقول للمطلوب وفي اشتراطه فلمن أثبته. وفي الظهيرية: إذا اختلفا في جنس المعقود عليه تحالفا وكذا في الصفة بخلاف الاختلاف في الصفة في بيع العين، ولو اختلفا في مكان الإيفاء فالقول للمطلوب، وإن برهنا فللطالب عنده وعندهما يتحالفان ويترادان السلم، وقيل على العكس وفي الصحاح: ردأ الشيء يردأ رداءة فهو رديء أي فاسد وأردأته أي أفسدته ا هـ. وقد ذكر المصنف رحمه الله تعالى أولاً في الدعوى التأجيل وفي النفي الأجل فظاهره أنه لا فرق بينهما عنده وليس كذلك لما في القاموس: الأجل غاية الوقت في الموت وحلول الدين ومدة الشيء والجمع آجال والتأجيل تحديد الأجل ا.هـ. والتحديد بمعنى التقدير وقدمنا أنهما لو اختلفا في مقداره فالقول للطالب فتعين أن يكون التأجيل في كلامه بمعنى الأجل مجازاً ا هـ. بدليل الثاني.
That the comprehensive description covering bad and good quality would be more appropriate because if one of them says 'we stipulated it good' and the other denies the stipulation entirely, then the word is of the affirmant. This is restricted to disputes over the origin of deferment because if they dispute over its amount, then the word is of the claimant with an oath due to his denial of the increase. And whichever presents proof is accepted, and if both present proof, judgment is given with the evidence of the defendant due to his establishing the increase. And if they dispute over its passing, then the word is of the defendant due to his denial of the claim being due. If they present proof, judgment is given with the evidence of the defendant for establishing the increase of the term. So the word is his word, i.e., the Muslim ilayh (buyer), and his evidence is his evidence. However, if we look at the form, he is the denier, and if we look at the meaning, its meaning is the establishment of the right in the future month. If both present evidence, then the evidence of the Muslim ilayh in its meaning establishes a right for him in a month that the evidence of the rabb al-salam (seller) did not address that month, so his evidence is more deserving. Likewise in Idah al-Kirmani. Then know that there is a difference between the term (ajal) and description (wasf): dispute over the amount of the term does not necessitate the mutual oath (tahaluf), while dispute over description necessitates it because it runs in the place of the original. In al-Khulasa: If in salam he stipulates a good garment, and he brings a garment and claims it is good while the claimant denies, then the judge brings two experts from that craft—this is more precautionary, and one suffices. If they say it is good, he compels him to accept. If they dispute in salam, they take mutual oaths by istihsan. One begins with the oath of the defendant according to Abu Yusuf, then he retracted and said with the oath of the claimant, and that is the saying of Muhammad. Whichever presents proof is accepted. If both present proof, judgment is given with the evidence of the rabb al-salam for a single salam according to Abu Yusuf, and it is said that is the saying of Abu Hanifa. The issue has three aspects: because the capital is either a specific item or a debt, and each aspect has three aspects: they agree on the capital and dispute over the subject of delivery, or over the counter-value, or dispute over both. If the capital is a specific item and they dispute over the subject of delivery only, and the claimant says 'this garment for a load of wheat' while the other says 'for half a load or for barley or for poor-quality wheat,' and they present evidence, judgment is given with the evidence of the rabb al-salam by consensus. If they dispute over the capital: one says 'this garment' and the other says 'this slave,' and they agree that the subject of delivery is wheat, or one says 'this garment for a load of wheat' and the other says 'for a load of barley,' and they present evidence, judgment is given for two salam contracts. So Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) is upon his principle, and Abu Yusuf says each claims a contract different from what the other claims. And if the capital is dirhams or dinars: if they agree on the capital but dispute over the subject of delivery and present evidence, then the evidence is for the rabb al-salam and judgment is given for one salam according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. If the dispute is over the counter-value, then it follows this difference. If they dispute over both: one says 'ten dirhams for two loads of wheat' and the other says 'fifteen for one load' and present evidence, then according to Abu Yusuf the increase is established, so fifteen for two loads is obligatory, and judgment is not given for two salam contracts, while according to Muhammad judgment is given for two salam contracts: a contract for fifteen for one load and a contract for ten for two loads. If one claims the capital is dirhams and the other dinars, this is not mentioned. And it is appropriate that the other stipulates good quality while the other says 'we only stipulated poor quality' and the intended is the first. Therefore he appended his saying 'no for us regarding description and term,' to indicate that poor quality is an example, so if one says 'we stipulated good' and the other says 'we did not stipulate anything,' then the ruling is that judgment is given for two salam contracts as in the case of two garments. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir. The conclusion is that if they dispute over genus, description, or amount, they take mutual oaths, whether regarding the capital or the subject of delivery. If they dispute over the stipulation of description or term, then the word is for the affirmant, not the denier. If they dispute over the amount of the term, then the word is for the rabb al-salam. If they dispute over its passing, then the word is for the Muslim ilayh. If they dispute over specifying the place of fulfillment, then the word is for the defendant, and if over its stipulation, then for the one who affirms it. In al-Zahiriyya: If they dispute over the genus of the subject of contract, they take mutual oaths, and likewise over description, unlike the dispute over description in the sale of a specific item. If they dispute over the place of fulfillment, the word is for the defendant, and if they present proof, then for the claimant according to him, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they take mutual oaths and return the salam. It is said the opposite. In al-Sihah: rada'a means decay, so radi' means corrupt, and arda'tuhu means I corrupted it. End. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned first in the claim 'ta'jil' and in the denial 'ajal,' so apparently there is no difference between them according to him, but it is not so because in al-Qamus: ajal is the ultimate time for death, payment of debt, and the duration of something, plural ajali, and ta'jil is specifying ajal. End. Specifying means determining, and we stated earlier that if they dispute over its amount, the word is for the claimant, so it is determined that ta'jil in his speech is used metaphorically as ajal. End. By the second evidence.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of disputes that may arise in a salam contract, focusing on disputes over description (wasf) and term (ajal). The author explains that when one party affirms a stipulation and the other denies, the burden of proof is on the affirmant. For disputes over the duration of the term, the claimant (rabb al-salam) is believed with an oath. If both present evidence, the defendant's evidence is preferred because they establish an increase. The text then distinguishes between disputes over the amount of the term (which do not lead to mutual oaths) and disputes over description (which do). Intra-Hanafi differences are noted: Abu Yusuf initially said the oath begins with the defendant, then retracted to the claimant's oath, which is also Muhammad's view. The author also discusses cases where the capital is a specific item versus debt, with Imam Muhammad asserting two separate contracts when both claim different contracts, while Abu Yusuf often rules for one contract with the increase established. The passage concludes with a linguistic clarification: 'ta'jil' (specifying a term) is often used metaphorically as 'ajal' (the term itself) in the context of the matn, based on the ruling that the claimant's word is accepted for the amount.

Key Terms
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital paid upfront in a salam contract; can be a specific item ('ayn) or a debt (dayn).
مسلم فيه (muslim fih): The subject matter to be delivered in a salam contract.
قلب (qalb): The counter-value or the capital itself; in context, 'al-qalb' refers to the capital (ra's al-mal) in dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract where the capital is a specific item (e.g., a garment) and the parties dispute over the capital itself (garment vs. slave) but agree on the subject matter (wheat), what is the ruling according to both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?
A) Both rule one contract.
B) Both rule two contracts.
C) Muhammad: two contracts; Abu Yusuf: each claims a different contract (effectively two).
D) Abu Yusuf: one contract; Muhammad: two contracts.

Key Takeaway
The number of salam contracts when both parties bring evidence depends on the type of capital and the focus of the dispute; Abu Yusuf generally rules one contract with increase, Muhammad rules two contracts, and the mufta bihi is often Abu Yusuf's view.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

8 min
Validity of Salam and Istisna' Contracts
Ruling on the validity of salam and istisna' with evidence and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on salam and istisna' by identifying their conditions, evidence, and the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
Salam is valid because its terms are determinable; istisna' is valid by practical consensus and istihsan, overriding the qiyas against selling non-existent objects.

This lesson explores two special sale contracts—salam and istisna'—that deal with future delivery. While salam is straightforward, istisna' involves a conflict between qiyas and istihsan, highlighting a key Hanafi methodology. We will analyze the conditions for each, their proof, and why istisna' is binding despite the general rule against selling what is not possessed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
First, note that both salam and istisna' are declared valid (sahih). Recognize that salam is a forward sale of fungible goods with full upfront payment, while istisna' is a contract to manufacture a specific item. Both involve future delivery but have distinct conditions.
Salam is explicitly allowed in the Quran (2:282) and by ijma; istisna' is based on practice.
2
Examine the proof for salam
The text states salam is valid because its subject matter can be fully described (ضبط الصفة) and quantity known (معرفة المقدار). This allows all conditions to be met, avoiding gharar. Thus, salam is a valid exception to the prohibition of selling non-existent objects.
Salam must have full payment at the time of contract; this is a condition.
3
Examine the proof for istisna'
The evidence for istisna' is practical consensus (الإجماع العملي) established by istihsan. This means continuous practice of Muslims across eras overrides the analogy (qiyas) that would otherwise forbid it. The text mentions Zufar's qiyas objection that istisna' is selling a non-existent object, but this is abandoned.
Istihsan is a Hanafi source of law that allows departure from qiyas when there is stronger evidence or custom.
4
Analyze the qiyas objection and Hanafi response
Zufar argued that istisna' is invalid by qiyas because it sells something that does not yet exist (bay' al-ma'dum). The Hanafi school responds that the ruling is based on istihsan due to practical consensus and widespread need. The text contrasts istisna' with mu'awama and muzara'a, which are not binding despite practice, because istisna' had consensus from the early generations.
This shows how Hanafi jurists weigh consensus and custom against strict analogy.
5
Determine the binding nature of istisna'
The text says 'wa qasarnahu' (we have restricted it to binding), meaning the later relied-upon position is that istisna' is a binding contract (lazim). Unlike Abu Hanifa's view on mu'awama and muzara'a, istisna' is enforceable once agreed.
This is the mufta bihi position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح السلم والاستصناع في نحو خف وطست أما السلم فلإمكان ضبط الصفة ومعرفة المقدار فكان سلماً باستجماع شرائطه، وأما الاستصناع فالكلام فيه في مواضع: الأول في معناه لغة فهو طلب الصنعة. وفي القاموس: الصناعة ككتابة حرفة الصانع وعمله الصنعة ا هـ. فعلى هذا الاستصناع لغة طلب عمل الصانع. وشرعاً أن يقول لصاحب خف أو مكعب أو صفاراً صنع لي خفاً طوله كذا وسعته كذا أو دستاً أي برمة تسع كذا ووزنها كذا على هيئة كذا بكذا ويعطي الثمن المسمى أو لا يعطي شيئاً فيقبل الآخر منه. الثاني في دليله وهو الإجماع العملي وهو ثابت بالاستحسان والقياس أن لا يجوز وهو قول زفر لكونه بيع المعدوم وتركناه للتعامل ولا تلزم المعاملة والمزارعة على قول أبي حنيفة مع التعامل لثبوت الخلاف فيهما في الصدر الأول، وهذا بالاتفاق فلهذا قصرناه. كذلك وبه اندفع ما في البحر.
His saying: (And salam and istisna' are valid in things like shoes and basins) — as for salam, because description can be specified and quantity can be known, so it is salam with all its conditions complete. As for istisna', the discussion about it is in several places: First, its linguistic meaning: it is requesting craftsmanship. In al-Qamus: 'Sina'a' (with fatha and sukun) is the craft of the artisan, and his work is 'sina'a'. End. Thus istisna' linguistically is requesting the work of the artisan. Legally, it is that he says to the owner of a shoe, or a cube [metalworker], or a brassworker: 'Make for me a shoe of such and such length and width, or a cooking pot (dast) [i.e., a pot] that holds such and such, with such and such weight, in such a shape, for such and such price,' and he gives the named price or does not give anything, and the other accepts from him. Second, its evidence: it is the practical consensus (ijma' 'amali) which is established by istihsan. By qiyas it is not permissible — this is the saying of Zufar because it is selling a non-existent object, but we have abandoned qiyas due to transaction practice. And the transaction of mu'awama (forward sale of fruits) and muzara'a (sharecropping) are not binding according to Abu Hanifa despite practice, due to the existence of disagreement about them in the early generation, while this (istisna') is by agreement, therefore we have restricted it (to binding effect). Likewise, by it what is in al-Bahr is repelled.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of salam (forward sale) and istisna' (commission to manufacture). The author begins by stating that salam is valid because its conditions can be fully specified: description, quantity, and term are determinable. Istisna' is then analyzed linguistically and legally. Linguistically, it is the act of requesting a craftsman to produce something. Legally, it is an agreement where the buyer commissions an artisan to make a specific item (like a shoe or pot) with exact measurements and price, with or without upfront payment. The primary evidence for istisna' is practical consensus (ijma' 'amali) based on continuous practice of Muslims, and it is validated by istihsan (juristic preference). The author contrasts it with qiyas (analogy) that would normally forbid it as selling a non-existent object (bay' al-ma'dum) — a position attributed to Zufar. However, the Hanafi school abandons that qiyas in favor of istihsan due to the widespread need. The text also notes that unlike mu'awama and muzara'a (which are not binding according to Abu Hanifa despite local custom), istisna' is binding by agreement in the early generations. The author then remarks that this explanation refutes a objection from al-Bahr.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisna'): A contract to manufacture a specific item according to agreed specifications.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: abandoning a qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence (like consensus or necessity).
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in full at the time of contract for goods to be delivered later, with specific description and quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the validity of istisna' according to the Hanafi school?
A) Direct Quranic verse
B) Qiyas on salam
C) Practical consensus (ijma' 'amali) supported by istihsan
D) A hadith from the Prophet ﷺ

Key Takeaway
Salam and istisna' are valid exceptions to the prohibition of selling non-existent objects, with istisna' upheld by istihsan over qiyas, and both are binding contracts in the Hanafi school.

Refining the Meaning of Ajal and Ta'jil
Critical refinement distinguishing ajal from ta'jil in salam disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ajal (duration) and ta'jil (specification) and apply the rule that in disputes over ta'jil, the one who claims a definite specification is given credence.

Core Idea
Ajal is the duration; ta'jil is the act of specifying. Disputes about ta'jil are about the existence of a term, not its length.

In Hanafi salam contracts, the delivery term (ajal) is a required condition. But what happens when the parties disagree on how the term was specified? This passage resolves a common confusion: the dispute is not about the length of the term but about the act of setting it (ta'jil). Understanding this distinction ensures correct application of the rule that the one who alleges a specific term is believed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Distinguish between 'ajal' (duration) and 'ta'jil' (act of specifying). The passage clarifies that they are not synonymous in legal analysis.
2
Locate the original claim
The earlier statement claimed that 'ta'jil' is used in the meaning of 'ajal'. Recognize this as the target of the critique.
3
Follow the critique
The commentator argues the opposite: 'ajal' should be understood as 'ta'jil' based on the context of the legal issue. The dispute is about specification, not duration.
The critique relies on the practical example of conflicting specifications.
4
Apply the distinction
If the dispute is over how the term was set (e.g., 'until wind blows' vs 'month'), it is a ta'jil dispute. The ruling is: the one who claims a definite specification is believed.
This is the 'qawl al-mudda'i' rule applied to specification.
5
Summarize the practical rule
In a salam term dispute, first categorize whether the disagreement is about duration (ajal) or specification (ta'jil). Only then apply the corresponding procedural rule.

قوله : فتعين أن يكون التأجيل في كلامه بمعنى الأجل) أقول : الظاهر تعين العكس. ثم رأيت في النهر : لا نسلم أنه يتعين ما ادعاه بل المناسب لوضع المسألة أن يكون الأجل بمعنى التأجيل حتى لو اختلفا في تجديده بأن قال أحدهما أجلناه إلى هبوب الريح وقال الآخر إلى شهر فالقول لمدعي التحديد، وأما ما ذكره فليس من المسألة في شيء فتدبره اهـ. أرى لأن بمعنى المدة والاختلاف فيها اختلاف في مقدارها وذلك ليس موضوع مسألة الكتاب، وأما الاختلاف في التأجيل فمعناه الاختلاف في التقرير والتحديد والاختلاف فيه اختلاف في أصل الأجل.
His saying: (So it is determined that ta'jil in his speech is in the meaning of ajal) — I say: The apparent is the opposite determination. Then I saw in al-Nahr: We do not accept that what he claimed is determined. Rather, what is appropriate for the context of the issue is that ajal is in the meaning of ta'jil, such that if they dispute over its renewal: one says 'we deferred it until the wind blows' and the other says 'until a month,' then the word is for the one who claims specification. As for what he mentioned, it is not part of the issue at all, so ponder it. End. I see it is because ajal means the duration, and the difference in it is a difference in its amount, and that is not the subject of the issue in the book. As for the difference in ta'jil, its meaning is the difference in the determination and specification, and the difference in it is a difference in the origin of the ajal.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from a later commentator (possibly from al-Nahr) on the earlier statement that 'ta'jil' is used metaphorically as 'ajal'. The commentator argues the opposite: 'ajal' should be understood as 'ta'jil' (the act of specifying) in the context of the original matn. This is because the dispute is about specifying the term, not about the duration itself. For example, if one says 'we deferred until the wind blows' and the other says 'until a month,' this is a dispute over the specification of the term, not over its length. The earlier author's attempt to equate ta'jil with ajal is therefore not applicable to the actual legal issue. The commentator insists that the correct interpretation is that ajal here means ta'jil, and the ruling that the claimant's word is accepted is about the specification of the term, not about its duration.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): The term or deadline for payment or delivery.
تأجيل (ta'jil): The act of setting or determining the ajal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator in al-Nahr, what is the correct interpretation of the term 'ajal' in the context of the original matn's dispute?
A) It means the duration of time
B) It means the act of specifying (ta'jil)
C) It is synonymous with 'mudda'
D) It refers to the amount of payment

Key Takeaway
In salam term disputes, correctly identifying whether the conflict is over duration (ajal) or specification (ta'jil) determines the applicable legal rule.
