Disputes in Salam Contract: Rulings and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Rulings for disputes in salam contracts with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings for each type of dispute in a salam contract and explain the practical difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions when both parties produce evidence.

Core Idea
In salam disputes, mutual oaths rescind the contract; only when one party affirms a stipulation do they avoid oaths.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the various scenarios of disagreement in salam contracts. It covers disputes over subject matter, terms, place of delivery, and a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two students when both parties produce evidence. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the salam contract and recognizing the practical consequence of differing scholarly opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of dispute
Determine what the parties disagree on: genus/description/quantity of the subject matter, stipulation of a description or term, length of term, passing of term, place of delivery, or its stipulation. Each has a specific ruling.
The ruling depends on whether the dispute is about an existing element (like genus) or a stipulation (like description).
2
Apply the default rule: mutual oaths (tahāluf)
If the dispute is about genus, description, or amount (quantity), the parties take mutual oaths and the contract is rescinded. This applies whether the dispute is about the capital or the subject matter.
Mutual oaths are a distinctive Hanafi remedy for contractual disputes with equal lack of evidence.
3
Apply the rule of affirmant's word for stipulations
If the dispute is about whether a description or term was stipulated, the word is given to the one who affirms it (claims it was stipulated), not the one who denies. Similarly for stipulation of a place of delivery.
The affirmant is the party insisting on the additional condition.
4
Determine default word for amount and passing of term and place
If they disagree on the length of the term, the buyer's word is taken. If on whether the term has passed, the seller's word. If on the place of delivery (without dispute on its stipulation), the defendant's word.
These shift the evidential burden based on who is more likely to know or who is being sued.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
When both parties produce evidence, Abu Hanifa gives the word to the plaintiff, while the Sahibayn rule that they take mutual oaths and rescind. This difference affects the outcome: under Abu Hanifa, one party wins; under Sahibayn, the contract collapses.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the mufta bihi is not specified in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقضي بسلمين كما في الثوبين ؛ كذا في فتح القدير والحاصل أنهما إن اختلفا في الجنس والصفة أو المقدار تحالفا، سواء كان في رأس المال أو في المسلم إليه. وإن اختلفا في اشتراط الوصف أو الأجل فالقول لمثبته لا النافيه، وإن اختلفا في مقدار الأجل فالقول لرب السلم، وإن اختلفا في مضيه فالقول للمسلم إليه، وإن اختلفا في بيان مكان الإيفاء فالقول للمطلوب وفي اشتراطه فلمن أثبته. وفي الظهيرية : إذا اختلفا في جنس المعقود عليه تحالفا وكذا في الصفة بخلاف الاختلاف في الصفة في بيع العين، ولو اختلفا في مكان الإيفاء فالقول للمطلوب، وإن برهنا فللطالب عنده وعندهما يتحالفان ويترادان السلم، وقيل على العكس وفي الصحاح : ردأ الشيء يردأ رداءة فهو رديء أي فاسد وأردأته أي أفسدته ا هـ . وقد ذكر المصنف رحمه الله تعالى أولاً في الدعوى التأجيل وفي النفي الأجل فظاهره أنه لا. فرق بينهما عنده وليس كذلك لما في القاموس : الأجل غاية الوقت في الموت وحلول الدين ومدة الشيء والجمع آجال والتأجيل تحديد الأجل ا.هـ. والتحديد بمعنى التقدير وقدمنا أنهما لو اختلفا في مقداره فالقول للطالب فتعين أن يكون التأجيل في كلامه بمعنى الأجل مجازاً اهـ.
It is judged as two salam contracts, like in the case of two garments; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The summary: if they differ in genus, description, or amount, they take mutual oaths, whether regarding the capital or the subject matter. If they differ regarding the stipulation of description or term, the word is of the one who affirms it, not the denier. If they differ regarding the amount of the term, the word is of the owner of the salam (the buyer). If they differ regarding its passing, the word is of the seller. If they differ regarding the place of delivery, the word is of the one being claimed from; and regarding its stipulation, the word is of the one who affirms it. In al-Zahiriyyah: if they differ regarding the genus of the subject matter, they take mutual oaths, and likewise for description, unlike the difference in description in the sale of a specific item. If they differ regarding the place of delivery, the word is of the one being claimed from. If they both produce evidence, then according to him (Abu Hanifa) the word is of the plaintiff; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they take mutual oaths and rescind the salam contract. And it is said the opposite. In al-Sihah: rada'a shay'un yardu'u rada'atan fahuwa radiy'un meaning bad; arada'tuhu meaning I spoiled it. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) first mentioned 'tajil' in the claim and 'ajal' in the denial, so apparently there is no difference between them according to him, but it is not so because in al-Qamus: al-ajal is the end time of death, the term of a debt, and the duration of something; its plural is ajal; and al-tajil is the determination of the term. Determination means estimation. We have stated that if they differ in its amount, the word is of the plaintiff, so it is determined that 'tajil' in his speech is used metaphorically for 'ajal'.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a sharh on Kanz al-Daqa'iq and presents rulings for disputes in salam contracts. The passage first summarizes: if the parties disagree on genus, description, or quantity, they perform mutual oaths (tahaluf) and the contract is dissolved. This applies whether the dispute is about the capital or the subject matter. If they disagree on whether a description or term was stipulated, the word is given to the one who affirms it (i.e., claims it was stipulated), not the one who denies. If they disagree on the length of the term, the buyer's word is taken. If they disagree on whether the term has passed, the seller's word is taken. Regarding the place of delivery, the word is of the defendant; if they disagree on whether a place was stipulated, the one who affirms it has the word. The passage then mentions a narration from al-Zahiriyyah: for genus and description, mutual oaths apply, unlike description in a specific item sale. For place of delivery, the defendant's word stands. A notable intra-Hanafi dispute arises when both parties bring evidence: Imam Abu Hanifa says the plaintiff's word is taken, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that they take mutual oaths and rescind. There is also a report of the opposite view. The passage concludes with a linguistic clarification from the author about the terms 'ajal' and 'tajil', showing that the author used 'tajil' metaphorically.

Key Terms
تحالفا (tahālafā): They take mutual oaths; each party swears to their claim, after which the contract is rescinded.
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The owner of the salam; the buyer who pays the capital in advance.
المسلم إليه (al-muslam ilayh): The seller who receives the capital and is obligated to deliver the goods at the specified term.
أجل (ajal): The term or deadline for delivery or payment.
تأجيل (tajil): The action of setting a term; determination of the deadline.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract dispute, if both parties produce evidence, what is the ruling according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)?
A) The plaintiff's word is taken.
B) The defendant's word is taken.
C) The parties take mutual oaths and rescind the contract.
D) The contract is void due to uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
In salam contract disputes, the ruling varies by the type of disagreement, and an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists when both parties present evidence: Abu Hanifa upholds the plaintiff's word, while the Sahibayn dissolve the contract through mutual oaths.

Validity of Istisna' (Manufacturing Contract) by Istihsan
Ruling with proof on istisna' validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istisna', explain its evidence from practical consensus and istihsan, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Zufr.

Core Idea
Istisna' is valid by istihsan because continuous custom overrides qiyas prohibiting sale of non-existent items.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a major Hanafi commentary, establishes the ruling on istisna' (manufacturing contract). It demonstrates how the Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to validate a transaction that would otherwise be invalid by qiyas (analogy). The key evidence is the practical consensus (ijma' amali) of the Muslim community, reflected in continuous custom. The text also addresses an intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufr, who rejects istisna' based on strict qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that istisna' is valid (sahih). First, note the legal category: it is a contract of sale (bay') but with unique features.
Istisna' is a contract for the manufacture of a specified item.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The evidence is stated as 'practical consensus' (ijma' amali) established by istihsan. Not explicit Quran or Sunnah, but the continuous practice of Muslims.
Hanafis accept practical consensus as a valid source in the absence of explicit text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal reasoning)
The reasoning: qiyas would prohibit istisna' because it involves selling something non-existent. However, istihsan allows it due to the widespread custom (ta'amul) of people. The custom is so pervasive that it amounts to consensus.
Istihsan here overrides qiyas, a standard Hanafi methodology.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufr ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent Hanafi scholar, holds istisna' invalid by qiyas. He analogizes it to selling a non-existent object, which is prohibited.
Zufr is a mujtahid within the Hanafi school; his view is recorded but not the relied-upon position.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The text indicates that the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is validity, based on istihsan and practical consensus. The tarjih authority is the continuous practice and the authors of the Hanafi corpus like al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
The mufta bihi matches the vast majority of Hanafi practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح السلم والاستصناع في نحو خف وطست أما السلم فلإمكان ضبط الصفة ومعرفة المقدار فكان سلماً باستجماع شرائطه، وأما الاستصناع فالكلام فيه في مواضع : الأول في معناه لغة فهو طلب الصنعة. وفي القاموس : الصناعة ككتابة حرفة الصانع وعمله الصنعة ا هـ. فعلى هذا الاستصناع لغة طلب عمل الصانع. وشرعاً أن بقول لصاحب خف أو مكعب أو صفاراً صنع لي خفاً طوله كذا وسعته كذا أو دستاً أي برمة تسع كذا ووزنها كذا على هيئة كذا بكذا ويعطي الثمن المسمى أو لا يعطي شيئاً فيقبل الآخر منه . الثاني في دليله وهو الإجماع العملي وهو ثابت بالاستحسان والقياس أن لا يجوز وهو قول زفر لكونه بيع المعدوم وتركناه للتعامل ولا تلزم المعاملة والمزارعة على قول أبي حنيفة مع التعامل لثبوت الخلاف فيهما في الصدر الأول، وهذا بالاتفاق فلهذا قصرناه كذلك وبه اندفع ما في البحر. قوله : فتعين أن يكون التأجيل في كلامه بمعنى الأجل) أقول : الظاهر تعين العكس. ثم رأيت في النهر : لا نسلم أنه يتعين ما ادعاه بل المناسب لوضع المسألة أن يكون الأجل بمعنى التأجيل حتى لو اختلفا في تجديده بأن قال أحدهما أجلناه إلى هبوب الريح وقال الآخر إلى شهر فالقول لمدعي التحديد، وأما ما ذكره فليس من المسألة في شيء فتدبره اهـ. أرى لأن بمعنى المدة والاختلاف فيها اختلاف في مقدارها وذلك ليس موضوع مسألة الكتاب، وأما الاختلاف في التأجيل فمعناه الاختلاف في التقرير والتحديد والاختلاف فيه اختلاف في أصل الأجل. ثم إنما كان ما ذكره في النهر من الاختلاف في التأجيل لأن التأجيل إلى هبوب الريح فاسد بمنزلة العدم تأمل قوله : (وفي القنية دفع مصحفاً إلى قوله لم يصح قال في النهر : وكأنه لعدم التعامل قوله : (لكن) قبل التسليم لا عند التسليم) قال في الكفاية : ولهذا يبطل بموت الصانع ولا يستوفي من تركته، ولو انعقد بيعاً ابتداء وانتهاء لكان لا يبطل بموته كما في بيع العين والسلم ويثبت له خيار الرؤية، ولو كان ينعقد عند التسليم لا قبله
His statement: (And salam and istisna' are valid in things like a khuff [sock] and a tist [basin]. As for salam, it is due to the possibility of specifying the description and knowing the amount, so it is a salam fulfilling its conditions. As for istisna', the discussion is in several places: First, its linguistic meaning: it is the request for craftsmanship. In al-Qamus: al-sana'ah (like kitabah) is the craft of the artisan, and his work is al-sun'ah. So istisna' linguistically is the request for the work of an artisan. Legally, it is that one says to a maker of khuffs or a coppersmith: 'Make for me a khuff of such length and width, or a pot of such capacity and weight, in such a form, for such price,' and he gives the named price or does not give anything, and the other accepts from him. Second, its evidence: it is practical consensus (ijma' amali), which is established by istihsan (juristic preference); qiyas would not permit it, and that is the view of Zufr because it is a sale of a non-existent item, but we left it due to people's practice. And the transaction of muzara'ah [sharecropping] and musaqat [irrigation contract] are not binding according to Abu Hanifa despite practice because there was disagreement about them in the early period, but this [istisna'] is by agreement, so we restricted it thus. And with this, what is in al-Bahr is refuted. His statement: 'So it is determined that 'tajil' in his speech is used metaphorically for 'ajal'' I say: the apparent is the opposite. Then I saw in al-Nahr: we do not accept that what he claimed is determined; rather, the appropriate for the subject of the issue is that 'ajal' is used in the sense of 'tajil', such that if they differ in its specification, e.g., one says we fixed it to the blowing of the wind and the other says to a month, then the word is of the one who claims specification. As for what he mentioned, it is not part of the issue at all, so reflect. I see that 'ajal' means duration, and the difference there is in the amount, which is not the subject of the book's issue; the difference in 'tajil' is difference in defining and specifying, and that difference is in the essence of the term. Moreover, what al-Nahr mentioned about the difference in 'tajil' is because fixing a term to the blowing of the wind is invalid, akin to non-existence. Consider his statement: (And in al-Qunyah: one gave a mushaf to a gilder to gild it with gold from his own, and showed him a sample of tenths, fifths, verse-headings, and surah beginnings, and ordered the mushaf to be gilded likewise for a known wage, it is not valid). He said in al-Nahr: It seems because of lack of practice. His statement: (but before delivery, not at delivery). He said in al-Kifayah: For this reason, it becomes void with the death of the artisan and is not collected from his estate. If it had been a sale from inception to completion, it would not become void with his death, as in the sale of a specific item and salam, and he would have the option of sight. If it were to be concluded at delivery, not before.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the validity of salam and istisna'. The author first affirms that salam is valid because the subject can be accurately described and quantified. For istisna' (manufacturing contract), he covers four aspects: (1) linguistic and legal definition; (2) evidence; (3) nature (whether it is a binding contract or a promise); (4) subject matter. The evidence for istisna' is practical consensus (al-ijma' al-'amali) and istihsan, overriding qiyas which would reject it as a sale of a non-existent item—this is the view of Zufr. Istisna' is deemed valid because of widespread custom (ta'amul), unlike sharecropping (muzara'ah) which remains non-binding according to Abu Hanifa due to early disagreement. The passage then includes a linguistic discussion on 'ajal' vs 'tajil' and concludes that the correct interpretation for the dispute scenario is that 'ajal' means the fixed term itself, not the act of fixing. Finally, it cites a case from al-Qunyah about gilding a mushaf which is invalid due to lack of custom, and explains why istisna' becomes void with the artisan's death—because it is like an ijarah (lease) in the beginning but a sale at the end, and before delivery it is not a complete sale.

Key Terms
استصناع (istiṣnāʿ): A manufacturing contract where one orders a craftsman to make a specific item with specified description and price, which may or may not be paid in advance.
إجماع عملي (ijmāʿ ʿamalī): Practical consensus derived from the continuous practice of Muslims, accepted as evidence in the absence of explicit textual proof.
مواعدة (muwāʿadah): A mutual promise, which is non-binding in law; distinguished from a binding contract (mu'āqadah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Analogy (qiyas) to salam
B) Explicit Quranic verse
C) Practical consensus (ijma' amali) and istihsan
D) Sayings of the Prophet (peace be upon him)

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is a valid contract in the Hanafi school, supported by practical consensus and istihsan, overriding qiyas that would render it invalid.

Istisna': A Valid Non-Binding Sale and the Term Dispute
Commentary on the legal nature and ruling of istisna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity and nature of istisna', explain the effect of setting a term, and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Istisna' is a non-binding sale validated by custom and istihsan; setting a term makes it salam per Abu Hanifa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a comprehensive analysis of the manufacturing contract (istisna'). It addresses its legal nature, object, bindingness, and the critical dispute over imposing a delivery term. We will unpack each component, focusing on the arguments that establish it as a sale and the implications of adding a fixed time.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of validity
Identify that istisna' is validated by widespread custom (ta'amul) and istihsan, not by strict qiyas, because the manufactured item does not exist at contract time.
The default qiyas would invalidate it due to gharar, but custom and necessity override.
2
Recognize the two views on nature
Some early Hanafis (e.g., al-Hakim al-Shahid) said it is a mere promise (muwa'idah). The relied-upon view is that it is a valid sale (bay'). Cite the evidence: Muhammad's use of qiyas and istihsan, which only apply to contracts; the specific custom; the option of sight; the craftsman's ownership of the price.
The passage states 'al-sahih min al-madhhab' for sale.
3
Identify the object of contract
The approved view (in al-Hidayah) is that the object is the commodity (al-'ayn), not the work. Evidence: the buyer's option of sight (he sees the item) and the permissibility of taking the item if made by another before the contract.
Al-Bardha'i argued for work, but the madhhab takes the commodity view.
4
Grasp the ruling: ja'iz ghayr lazim
Istisna' is permissible but not binding. Both parties may withdraw until the orderer accepts the finished product. The craftsman can even sell the item to a third party before the orderer sees it.
Non-bindingness is due to the need for flexibility; it resembles ijarah before acceptance.
5
Analyze the term dispute
Abu Hanifa: setting a fixed term (ajal) converts istisna' into salam. Sahibayn: if custom supports a term, it remains istisna'; otherwise it becomes salam. The passage prefers Abu Hanifa's view because salam is established by primary texts, while istisna' is based on custom.
Practical difference: salam requires full price at session, no option of sight; istisna' retains these.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رب على بيع سلمة وصاحب على ما فيه تعامل وفيما لا تعامل فيه رجعنا فيه إلى القياس كأن يستصنع حائكاً أو خياطاً لينسج له أو يخيط له قميصاً بغزل نفسه. وفي القنية: دفع مصحفاً إلى مذهب ليذهبه بذهب من عنده وأراه الذهب أنموذجاً من الأعشار والأخماس ورؤوس الآي وأوائل السور فأمره المصحف أن يذهبه كذلك بأجرة معلومة لا يصح. سئل عمر النسقي عمن دفع إلى حائك غزلاً لينسج له عمامة من سداه فجاء بها منسوجة فقال صاحب الغزل اشتريت منك ما في هذا المنسوج من الإبريسم بكذا وقال الآخر بعت هل يصح؟ فقال: ما صار على الأمر للمأمور من الإبريسم السدا بالعقد الأول صار ملكاً للآمر. قال أبو الفضل: الإبريسم دين الأمر وأجرة العمل عليه. قال النجار ابن لي بيتاً فإذا بنيته يقومه المقومون فما يقولون أدفعه إليك فرضيا به وبناه وقومه رجل باتفاقهما وأبى الصانع فله أجر مثله. وقال أبو حامد وحمير الوبري: هو بمنزلة المقوم لا الحكم فلا يلزمه تقويمه ا هـ. الثالث في صفته فقد اختلفوا في كونه مواعدة أو معاقدة فالحاكم الشهيد والصفار ومحمد بن المنشور مواعدة وإنما ينعقد عند الفراغ بالتعاطي ولهذا كان للصانع أن لا يعمل ولا يجبر عليه بخلاف السلم، وللمستصنع أن لا يقبل ما يأتي به ويرجع عنه، والصحيح من المذهب جوازه بيعاً لأن محمداً ذكر فيه القياس والاستحسان وهما لا يجريان في المواعدة، ولأن جوازه فيما فيه تعامل خاصة ولو كان مواعدة لجاز في الكل وسماه أيضاً شراء فقال إذا رآه المستصنع فله الخيار لأنه اشترى ما لم يره ولأن الصانع يملك الدراهم بقبضها ولو كانت مواعدة لم يملكها، وإثبات أبي اليسر الخيار لكل منهما لا يدل على أنه غير بيع كما في بيع المقايضة، وحين لزم جوازه علمنا أن الشارع اعتبر فيه المعدوم موجوداً وهو كثير في الشرع كطهارة صاحب العذر وتسمية الذابح إذا نسيها والرهن بالدين الموعود وقراءة المأموم. والرابع في المعقود عليه فاختلف فيه فالمذهب المرضي في الهداية أنه العين دون العمل. وقال البردعي: المعقود عليه العمل دون العين لأن الاستصناع ينبئ عنه. والأديم والصرم بمنزلة الصبغ والدليل على المذهب ما ذكرناه من قول محمد لأنه اشترى ما لم يره ولذا لو جاء به مفروغاً لا من صنعته أو من صنعته قبل العقد فأخذه جاز، وإنما يبطله بموت الصانع لشبهه بالإجارة. وفي الذخيرة هو إجارة ابتداء بيع انتهاء لكن قبل التسليم لا عند التسليم بدليل قولهم إذا مات الصانع يبطل ولا يستوفي المصنوع من تركته؛ ذكره محمد في كتاب البيوع. وإنما لم يجبر الصانع على العمل والمستصنع على إعطاء المسمى لأنه لا يمكنه إلا بإتلاف عين ماله والإجارة تفسخ بهذا العذر. الخامس في حكمه وهو الجواز دون اللزوم لأن جوازه للحاجة وهي في الجواز لا اللزوم ولذا قلنا للصانع أن يبيع المصنوع قبل أن يراه المستصنع لأن العقد غير لازم، وأما بعد ما رآه فالأصح أنه لا خيار للصانع بل إذا قبله المستصنع أجبر على دفعه له لأنه بالآخرة بائع. له وتفرع على عدم لزومه ما في فتاوى قاضيخان من الدعوى: رجل استصنع رجلاً في شيء ثم اختلفا في المصنوع فقال المستصنع لم تفعل ما أمرتك به وقال فعلت قالوا: لا يمين فيه لأحدهما على الآخر. ولو ادعى الصانع على رجل أنك استصنعت إلي في كذا وأنكر المدعى عليه لا يحلف ا هـ. قوله: (وله الخيار أي للمستصنع الخيار إذا رأى المصنوع) لما قدمناه أنه اشترى ما لم يره بخلاف السلم لأنه لا فائدة في إثبات الخيار فيه لأنه كلما رده عليه أعطاه غيره لكونه غير متعين إذا المسلم فيه في الذمة فيبقى فيها إلى أن يقبضه قيد به لأنه لا خيار للصانع لأنه باع ما لم يره. وعن أبي حنيفة أن له الخيار لأنه يلحقه الضرر بقطع الصرم والصحيح الأول قوله: (وللصانع بيعه قبل أن يراه أي المستصنع) لأنه لا يتعين إلا باختياره. قيد بقوله «قبل أن لأنه إذا رآه ورضي به امتنع على الصانع بيعه لأنه بالإحضار أسقط خيار ولزم قوله: (ومؤجله سلم) أي إذا أجله المستصنع صار سلماً وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا: إن ضرب الأجل فيما فيه تعامل فهو استصناع، وإن ضرب فيما لا تعامل فيه فهو سلم لتعذر جعله استصناعاً ويحمل الأجل فيما فيه تعامل على الاستعجال. وله أن يحتمل السلم فحمل عليه وهو أولى لكونه ثابتاً بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع مطلقاً. وأما الاستصناع فبالتعامل ومخصوص بما فيه تعامل، ولأن الأجل لتأخير المطالبة وذلك باللزوم وهو في السلم دونه. والمراد بالأجل ما من أن أقله شهر فإن لم يصلح كان استصناعاً إن جرى فيه تعامل وإلا ففاسد إن ذكره على وجه الاستمهال، فإن كان للاستعجال بأن قال على أن تفرغ منه غداً أو بعد غد كان صحيحاً. وفصل الهندواني فجعله من المستصنع استعجالاً ومن الصانع تعجيلاً. ثم فائدة كونه سلماً أن يشترط فيه شرائطه من القبض قبل الافتراق وعدم الخيار إلى غير ذلك من قدمه بساعة لم يثبت خيار الرؤية لأنه يكون مشترياً ما رآه وتمامه فيه. وفي نور العين في إصلاح جامع الفصولين نقلاً عن فتاوى ظهير الدين وينعقد إجارة ابتداء وبيعاً انتهاء متى سلم حتى لو مات الصانع قبل التسليم بطل ولا يستوفي المصنوع من تركته وينعقد بيعاً عند التسليم حتى لو سلم يثبت خيار الرؤية. ثم نقل بعده عبارة الذخيرة ثم قال: فبين ما في الكتابين تعارض ولعل الصواب هو الأول كما لا يخفى على من تأمل. اهـ. قوله: (وفي المغرب الطشت مؤنثة الخ) قال الرملي قال ابن
…on what has custom (ta'amul) and what has no custom, we return to analogy (qiyas), such as ordering a weaver or tailor to weave or sew a shirt with his own yarn. In al-Qunyah: if one gives a mushaf to a gilder to gild it with gold from the gilder and shows him the gold as a sample of tenths, fifths, verse headings, and chapter openings, and orders him to gild it likewise for a known wage, it is not valid. Abu al-Nasafi was asked about one who gave yarn to a weaver to weave a turban from its warp, and he brought it woven, and the yarn-owner said 'I bought from you the silk in this woven fabric for such-and-such' and the other said 'I sold' – is it valid? He said: whatever of the warp silk passed to the orderer by the first contract became the orderer's property. Abu al-Fadl said: the silk is a debt of the orderer and the work wage is on him. A builder said: build me a house, and when you build it, appraisers will appraise it, and whatever they say I will give you, and they agreed, and he built it, and a man appraised it by their agreement, and the craftsman refused, so he gets his equivalent wage. Abu Hamid and Humayr al-Wabari said: it is like the appraiser, not the judge, so he is not forced to accept the appraisal. End. Third: regarding its description; they differed on whether it is a promise (muwa'idah) or a contract (mu'aqadah). Al-Hakim al-Shahid, al-Saffar, and Muhammad ibn al-Manshur said it is a promise, and it only becomes binding upon completion by mutual taking (ta'ati), therefore the craftsman may not work and is not forced to, unlike salam, and the orderer may not accept what is brought and may withdraw. The sound view of the madhhab is that it is valid as a sale because Muhammad mentioned analogy and istihsan, and both do not apply to a promise, and because its validity is specifically in what has custom; if it were a promise, it would be valid in all cases. He also called it a purchase, saying: when the orderer sees it, he has the option because he bought something unseen; and the craftsman owns the dirhams upon receiving them; if it were a promise, he would not own them. Abu al-Yusr's establishing the option for each does not indicate it is not a sale, just as in barter. Since its validity has been established, we know that the Lawgiver considered the non-existent as existent, and this is frequent in the Law, like the purity of the excused person, the naming by the slaughterer if he forgets, the pledge for a promised debt, and the follower's recitation. Fourth: regarding the object of contract; they differed: the approved view in al-Hidayah is that it is the commodity (al-'ayn) not the work. al-Bardha'i said the object is the work, not the commodity, because istisna' indicates it. The hide and the piece [of leather] are like dye. The proof for the madhhab is what we mentioned from Muhammad's words: 'because he bought what he did not see'; thus if he brought it completed not from his workmanship or from his workmanship before the contract and he took it, it is permissible; it is only invalidated by the craftsman's death due to its resemblance to ijarah. In al-Dhakhirah, it is ijarah in the beginning and sale at the end, but before delivery, not at delivery, as evidenced by their statement: if the craftsman dies, it is invalidated and the manufactured item is not taken from his estate; Muhammad mentioned this in Kitab al-Buyu'. The craftsman is not forced to work, nor the orderer to give the named price, because that would require destroying his own property, and ijarah is rescinded due to this excuse. Fifth: regarding its ruling: it is permissible (jawaz) not binding (luzum), because its permissibility is for need, and need lies in permissibility not binding. Hence we said the craftsman may sell the manufactured item before the orderer sees it, because the contract is not binding. After the orderer sees it, the correct view is that the craftsman has no option; rather if the orderer accepts it, he is compelled to hand it over, because ultimately he is a seller. Derived from its non-binding nature is what is in Fatawa Qadikhan regarding claims: if a man orders another to manufacture something, then they dispute about the manufactured item, the orderer saying 'you did not do as I ordered' and the craftsman saying 'I did', they said: no oath is required from either against the other. And if the craftsman claims against a man that he ordered something, and the defendant denies, he is not made to take an oath. End. His saying: (and he has the option, i.e., the orderer has the option when he sees the manufactured item) because we established that he bought what he did not see, unlike salam because there is no benefit in establishing the option there, since whenever he returns it, he gives him another because it is not specified; the salam item is in the dhimmah and remains there until he receives it. This restricts it because the craftsman has no option since he sold what he did not see. From Abu Hanifa that he has the option because harm befalls him by cutting the hide; the first is correct. His saying: (and the craftsman may sell it before he sees it, i.e., the orderer) because it is not specified except by his choice. Restricted by his saying 'before', because when he sees it and is pleased with it, it becomes forbidden for the craftsman to sell it, because by presenting it he dropped his option and it becomes binding. His saying: (and its term makes it salam) i.e., if the orderer sets a term, it becomes salam – this according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: if the term is set in what has custom, it is istisna', and if set in what has no custom, it is salam because it cannot be made istisna'. The term in what has custom is interpreted as urging haste. It is also possible to consider it salam, so it is interpreted as such, and this is preferable because salam is established by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus absolutely, whereas istisna' is by custom and restricted to what has custom, and because the term is for deferring the claim, and that requires bindingness, which is present in salam more than in istisna'. What is meant by the term is that its minimum is one month; if it does not suffice, it is istisna' if custom exists, otherwise it is invalid if mentioned as a grace period. If it is for urging haste, e.g., 'on condition that you finish it tomorrow or the day after', it is valid. al-Hinduwani distinguished: from the orderer it is urging haste, from the craftsman it is expediting. Then the benefit of it being salam is that its conditions are required: taking possession before separation, no option, etc. Even if he advances it by an hour, the option of sight is not established because he is buying what he has seen; the full details are there. In Nur al-'Ayn fi Islah Jami' al-Fusulain, quoting from Fatawa Zahir al-Din: it becomes ijarah in the beginning and a sale at the end when delivered, such that if the craftsman dies before delivery it is invalidated and the manufactured item is not taken from his estate, and it becomes a sale at delivery such that if he delivers, the option of sight is established. Then he quoted the wording of al-Dhakhirah and said: there is a contradiction between the two books, and perhaps the first is correct, as is clear to whoever reflects. End. His saying: (And in al-Mughrib, the basin is feminine, etc.) al-Ramli said Ibn…
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the rulings of istisna' (manufacturing or order-to-manufacture contract). The passage covers five main points: the basis of validity (practice and analogy), the legal nature (whether it is a binding contract or a mere promise), the object of contract (the commodity or the work), the ruling (permissible but non-binding), and the effect of setting a fixed term (making it a salam contract). The author explains that istisna' is validated by widespread custom (ta'amul) and istihsan (juristic preference), even though the object does not exist at the time of contract. The Hanafi school considers it a valid sale (not a promise) because Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani applied both qiyas (analogy) and istihsan, which do not apply to mere promises. The buyer gets the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) because he bought something unseen. The contract is not binding (ja'iz ghayr lazim), meaning both parties may withdraw until the buyer accepts the manufactured item. In case of a disagreement about the specifications, neither party is required to take an oath. If a fixed term is set (e.g., one month), the contract becomes salam according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that it remains istisna' if custom supports it. The passage also discusses the effect of the craftsman's death: it nullifies the contract, resembling ijarah. Overall, the Hanafi position balances between strict qiyas (which would reject the contract due to uncertainty) and the practical needs of people, leaning on custom.

Key Terms
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract whereby a person orders a craftsman to manufacture a specific item from the craftsman's own material, for a specified price.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for a well-described commodity to be delivered at a future date.
المواعدة (al-muwa'idah): A mutual promise that is not legally binding; each party may withdraw without penalty.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of sight: the buyer has the right to cancel the sale if he has not seen the purchased item and later sees it and dislikes it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a fixed term (e.g., one month) is set in an istisna' contract?
A) It remains istisna', and the orderer retains the option of sight.
B) It becomes salam, and the price must be paid at the contract session.
C) The contract becomes void due to uncertainty.
D) It becomes an ijarah contract for the work.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is a non-binding sale validated by custom; setting a fixed term transforms it into a salam contract according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Istisna' and the Effect of Setting a Term
Explaining the non-binding nature of istisna' and its transformation into salam when an ajl is set

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istisna' (permissible and non-binding), explain the option of inspection, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute over the effect of setting a term.

Core Idea
Istisna' is permissible but not binding; setting a term transforms it into binding salam per Abu Hanifa.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the legal nature of the manufacturing contract (istisna'). It explains why the contract is not binding, the customer's option of inspection, and a key dispute among the Hanafi imams about whether setting a deferred payment term turns the contract into salam. Understanding this distinction is critical for modern contractual arrangements where custom orders are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State what istisna' is and its hukm level (ja'iz vs lazim). The author says it is permissible but not binding.
The ruling is from the fifth point in the passage.
2
Understand the option of inspection
Recognize that the customer has khiyar al-ru'yah because he bought an unseen item. Note that the craftsman generally has no option after the customer sees the product.
Abu Hanifa had a different view but the correct view is that only the customer has the option.
3
Analyze the effect of setting a term (ajl)
Examine the dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that setting a term makes the contract salam; the Sahibayn distinguish based on custom.
The commentator prefers Abu Hanifa's view because salam is established by stronger evidence.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
If it is salam, conditions like delivery before parting apply and no option of inspection exists. If it remains istisna', it is non-binding and the customer retains the option.
This practical consequence affects contract enforceability.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern dispute
Consider a scenario: a customer orders a custom piece of furniture with payment in one month. Per Abu Hanifa, it becomes salam; per the Sahibayn, if such term is customary, it remains istisna'. Determine which view is mufta bihi.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مات الصانع يبطل ولا يستوفي المصنوع من تركته؛ ذكره محمد في كتاب البيوع. وإنما لم يجبر الصانع على العمل والمستصنع على إعطاء المسمى لأنه لا يمكنه إلا بإتلاف عين ماله والإجارة تفسخ بهذا العذر. الخامس في حكمه وهو الجواز دون اللزوم لأن جوازه للحاجة وهي في الجواز لا اللزوم ولذا قلنا للصانع أن يبيع المصنوع قبل أن يراه المستصنع لأن العقد غير لازم، وأما بعد ما رآه فالأصح أنه لا خيار للصانع بل إذا قبله المستصنع أجبر على دفعه له لأنه بالآخرة بائع له. وتفرع على عدم لزومه ما في فتاوى قاضيخان من الدعوى: رجل استصنع رجلاً في شيء ثم اختلفا في المصنوع فقال المستصنع لم تفعل ما أمرتك به وقال فعلت قالوا: لا يمين فيه لأحدهما على الآخر. ولو ادعى الصانع على رجل أنك استصنعت إلي في كذا وأنكر المدعى عليه لا يحلف. ا هـ.

قوله: (وله الخيار أي للمستصنع الخيار إذا رأى المصنوع) لما قدمناه أنه اشترى ما لم يره بخلاف السلم لأنه لا فائدة في إثبات الخيار فيه لأنه كلما رده عليه أعطاه غيره لكونه غير متعين إذا المسلم فيه في الذمة فيبقى فيها إلى أن يقبضه. قيد به لأنه لا خيار للصانع لأنه باع ما لم يره. وعن أبي حنيفة أن له الخيار لأنه يلحقه الضرر بقطع الصرم والصحيح الأول.

قوله: (وللصانع بيعه قبل أن يراه) أي المستصنع لأنه لا يتعين إلا باختياره. قيد بقوله "قبل أن" لأنه إذا رآه ورضي به امتنع على الصانع بيعه لأنه بالإحضار أسقط خياره ولزم.

قوله: (ومؤجله سلم) أي إذا أجله المستصنع صار سلماً وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا: إن ضرب الأجل فيما فيه تعامل فهو استصناع، وإن ضرب فيما لا تعامل فيه فهو سلم لتعذر جعله استصناعاً ويحمل الأجل فيما فيه تعامل على الاستعجال. وله أن يحتمل السلم فحمل عليه وهو أولى لكونه ثابتاً بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع مطلقاً. وأما الاستصناع فبالتعامل ومخصوص بما فيه تعامل، ولأن الأجل لتأخير المطالبة وذلك باللزوم وهو في السلم دونه. والمراد بالأجل ما من أن أقله شهر فإن لم يصلح كان استصناعاً إن جرى فيه تعامل وإلا ففاسد إن ذكره على وجه الاستمهال، فإن كان للاستعجال بأن قال على أن تفرغ منه غداً أو بعد غد كان صحيحاً. وفصل الهندواني فجعله من المستصنع استعجالاً ومن الصانع تعجيلاً. ثم فائدة كونه سلماً أن يشترط فيه شرائطه من القبض قبل الافتراق وعدم الخيار إلى غير ذلك من قدمه بساعة لم يثبت خيار الرؤية لأنه يكون مشترياً ما رآه وتمامه فيه.
If the craftsman dies, the contract becomes void and the manufactured item cannot be collected from his estate; Muhammad mentioned this in Kitab al-Buyu'. The craftsman is not compelled to work nor the customer to pay the named price because that would only be possible by destroying the customer's property, and an ijarah contract is dissolved by this excuse. The fifth [point] concerns its ruling: it is permissible (ja'iz) but not binding, because its permissibility is for need, and need is met by permissibility not bindingness. Hence we say the craftsman may sell the manufactured item before the customer sees it, because the contract is not binding. After the customer sees it, however, the correct view is that the craftsman has no option; rather, if the customer accepts it, the craftsman is compelled to deliver it because ultimately he is a seller. Derived from its non-bindingness is what appears in the Fatawa of Qadikhan regarding claims: If a man commissions another for something, then they dispute about the product—the customer says 'you did not do what I ordered' and the craftsman says 'I did'—they said no oath is required from either. And if the craftsman claims against a man 'you commissioned me for such-and-such' and the defendant denies, he is not made to swear. End.

His statement: 'He has an option (i.e., the customer has an option when he sees the product)': because as we mentioned, he bought what he did not see, unlike salam because there is no benefit in establishing an option there, since whenever he returns it, the seller gives him another, as it is not specific (the item is in the dhimmah remains until he receives it). He restricted it because the craftsman has no option, as he sold what he did not see. From Abu Hanifa is the view that he has an option because he suffers harm by being cut off from payment, but the correct view is the first.

His statement: 'The craftsman may sell it before the customer sees it' i.e., the customer, because it is not specified except by his choice. He restricted by saying 'before' because if he has seen it and approved it, the craftsman is prevented from selling it because by presenting it he dropped his option and it became binding.

His statement: 'And if delayed, it is salam' i.e., if the customer delays payment by setting a term, it becomes salam. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If the term is set in what is customarily traded, it is istisna'; if set in what is not customarily traded, it is salam, due to inability to make it istisna'; and the term in what is customarily traded is construed as a request for haste. And it is possible that it admits both salam and istisna', so it is attributed to salam, which is preferable because salam is established by the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma' absolutely. Istisna' is based on custom and is restricted to what is customarily traded. Also because the term is for deferring demand, which requires bindingness, and bindingness is in salam more than istisna'. The term intended: its minimum is a month. If it does not fit, it is istisna' if customarily traded; otherwise it is void if mentioned as a deferment. If for haste, e.g., 'to be finished tomorrow or the day after,' it is valid. Al-Hindwani distinguished: from the customer it is haste, from the craftsman it is acceleration. Then the benefit of its being salam is that its conditions are required: delivery before parting, no option, etc. If a term is set even by an hour, the option of inspection is not established because he becomes a buyer of what he saw, and the completion is there.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on a text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It discusses the legal ruling (hukm) of the istisna' (manufacturing/commission) contract. The passage type is a ruling with proof and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author first establishes that if the craftsman dies, the contract becomes void because it is a contract that requires personal work, and the product cannot be taken from his estate. Then he explains why neither party can be compelled: compelling the craftsman to work would require destroying the customer's property (since the materials belong to the customer), and that would dissolve the ijarah aspect. Next, he states the fifth point: the contract is ja'iz (permissible) but not lazim (binding). This is because istisna' is allowed for need, and need is satisfied by permissibility, not bindingness. Consequently, the craftsman may sell the product to someone else before the customer sees it, but after the customer sees and accepts it, the craftsman is obliged to deliver. From this non-bindingness, a legal issue from Qadikhan's Fatawa follows: if they dispute about the work, no oath is required. The commentary then explains the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) for the customer: the customer has the right to reject after seeing the product because he bought it unseen. This is unlike salam, where the item is in the dhimmah and not specific, so rejection would have no practical benefit. The author notes that Abu Hanifa held an opposing view that the craftsman also has an option, but the correct view is that only the customer has it. The craftsman may sell the product before the customer sees it, but once the customer has seen and approved, the craftsman loses that right. Finally, there is a detailed discussion about the effect of setting a term (ajl). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a term is set for payment, the contract becomes salam. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) distinguish based on custom: if the term is set in a trade where istisna' is customary, it remains istisna' (and the term is for haste); if not customary, it becomes salam. The commentator prefers the view that it becomes salam because salam is established by stronger evidence (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma'). The minimum term for salam is one month; otherwise, the contract is either istisna' or void. If the term is for acceleration (e.g., a short period), the contract is valid as istisna'. Al-Hindwani's distinction is mentioned: from the customer it is a request for haste, from the craftsman it is an offer of acceleration. Once the term makes it salam, the conditions of salam apply, such as delivery before parting and no option of inspection even if the term is very short.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisnāʿ): A contract of manufacturing or commissioning where a customer orders a craftsman to make a specific item from materials provided by the customer (or where the craftsman uses his own materials).
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the full price is paid in advance for a specified fungible good to be delivered at a future date.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ruʾyah): The option of inspection: the right of the buyer (or customer in istisna') to confirm or reject the commodity upon actually seeing it, because it was bought unseen.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or period for payment or delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a customer sets a deferred payment term in an istisna' contract, what is the ruling per Abu Hanifa?
A) It remains istisna' and is non-binding.
B) It becomes a salam contract and becomes binding.
C) The contract becomes void (fasid) because terms cannot be set.
D) It becomes a regular sale (bay') and the customer loses the option.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is a non-binding contract; the customer retains the option of inspection until acceptance, and setting a term may convert it into the binding contract of salam per Abu Hanifa's preferred view.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Validity of Selling Dogs, Predators, and Birds
Ruling with proof on sale of animals and dhimmi capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the validity of selling dogs, predators, and birds, and identify the proof and the key conditions.

Core Idea
A dog is pure and its benefit is permitted, so its sale is valid — unlike the pig which is impure and without permitted use.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter of miscellaneous matters with a clear ruling: the sale of dogs, leopards, other predators, and birds (trained for hunting) is valid. The commentator provides both a transmitted proof and a rational argument, and addresses an intra-Hanafi disagreement about the dog's impurity. The lesson also touches on the dhimmi's capacity to sell, excluding wine and pigs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the central ruling: the sale of a dog, leopard, predators, and birds is valid (sahih). Note that the passage includes the dhimmi's status: he is like a Muslim except for wine and pigs.
The ruling applies specifically to animals that have a permitted benefit, such as hunting or guarding.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The text cites a narration from Abu Hanifa that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted taking a price for a hunting dog. This is a transmitted proof (dalil naqli). Also, the author gives a rational proof: the dog is valuable property (māl mutaqawwim) as a tool for hunting, and since the Lawgiver permitted its use, sale is likewise permitted.
The proof is considered zanni in transmission and meaning, but sufficient for a ruling of validity.
3
Analyze the concept of māl mutaqawwim
Understand that an object must have permissible benefit (manfaʿah mubāḥah) to be considered valuable property. The dog's benefit for hunting and guarding is permitted, so it is māl mutaqawwim, making its sale valid even if it were impure.
Contrast with wine and pigs: they have no permitted benefit, so they are not māl mutaqawwim.
4
Address the najāsah objection
The commentator explains that even if one holds the dog to be impure (as some Hanafi narrations suggest), impurity only forbids eating, not sale. Prohibition of sale is based on prohibition of benefit; since the dog's benefit is permitted, its sale is allowed, similar to the sale of dung (sarqīn).
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) opinion is that the dog is pure, so the objection does not arise.
5
Examine the dhimmi's capacity
The dhimmi is treated like a Muslim in selling, except for wine and pigs. This is because these items are completely prohibited and have no permissible benefit, even for the dhimmi to sell to a Muslim.
This ruling aligns with the Hanafi principle that contracts of dhimmis are valid for most transactions, but not for items that are ḥarām in themselves.

Source Text

في نسخة الزيلعي. وفي نسخة العيني مسائل متفرقة وعبر عنها بمسائل منثورة والمعنى واحد وحاصلها أن المسائل التي تشذ عن الأبواب المتقدمة فلم تذكر فيها إذا استدركت سميت بها أي متفرقة من أبواب أو منثورة عن أبوابها قوله: (صح بيع الكلب والفهد والسباع والطيور) لما رواه أبو حنيفة رضي الله تعالى عنه أنه رخص في ثمن كلب الصيد، ولأنه مال متقوم آلة الاصطياد فصح بيعه كالبازي بدليل أن الشارع أباح الانتفاع به حراسة واصطياداً فكذا بيعاً. وهذا على القول المفتى به من طهارة عينه بخلاف الخنزير فإنه نجس العين، وأما على رواية أنه نجس العين كالخنزير فقال في فتح القدير: ولو سلم نجاسة عينه فهي توجب حرمة أكله لا منع بيعه بل منع البيع بمنع الانتفاع شرعاً ولهذا أجزنا بيع السرقين والبعر مع نجاسة عينهما لإطلاق الانتفاع بهما عندنا بخلاف العذرة لم يطلق الانتفاع بها فمنع بيعها، فإن ثبت شرعاً إطلاق الانتفاع بها مخلوطة بالتراب ولو بالاستهلاك كالاستصباح بالزيت النجس كما قيل جاز بيع ذلك التراب التي هي في ضمنه...
Chapter of Miscellaneous Matters

The sale of a dog, leopard, predators, birds is valid, and a dhimmi (non-Muslim protected person) is like a Muslim in selling except wine and pigs, even if [the buyer] is a Muslim. In Al-Sihah: al-tast (طست) is al-tass (طس) in the dialect of Tayy, with one of the two s's replaced by a tā for ease; when you pluralize or diminutize, the s is restored because you separate them with an alif or yā, so you say tisās (طساس) and tisīs (طسيس) end. In Al-Mughrib: al-tast is feminine, it is foreign, and al-tass is its Arabization; the plural is tisās and tusūs; sometimes al-tasūt is mentioned, listed under the letter shīn, and al-qumqumah (with damm) is well-known. Al-Asma'i said: it is Roman, plural qamāqim; as in Al-Sihah. End. And Allah knows best.

Thus
Chapter of Miscellaneous Matters
Al-Hidayah: In the copy of al-Zayla'i, and in the copy of al-'Ayni, 'Miscellaneous Questions' or 'Scattered Questions' are used, meaning the same. Their summary is that questions that deviate from the previous chapters and were not mentioned there, when collected, are called by this name, i.e., 'miscellaneous from the chapters' or 'scattered from their chapters.' His statement: (The sale of a dog, leopard, predators, and birds is valid) due to what Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated: that he permitted the price of a hunting dog, and because it is valuable property (māl mutaqawwim) as a tool for hunting, its sale is valid like a falcon, evidenced that the Lawgiver permitted using it for guarding and hunting, so likewise its sale. This is according to the fatwa that its essence is pure, unlike the pig which is impure in essence. As for the narration that it is impure in essence like the pig, it was said in Fath al-Qadir: Even if its impurity were granted, it would only forbid eating it, not its sale; rather prohibition of sale is due to prohibition of benefit (intifa') by Shari'ah. Hence we permitted the sale of dung (sarqīn) and animal droppings (ba'r) despite their impurity because the benefit of them is permitted in our madhhab, unlike human waste ('adhirah) because its benefit was not permitted; so its sale is prohibited. If it is established by Shari'ah that benefit of it (mixed with soil) is permitted, even by consumption like using impure oil for lamps as is said, then the sale of that soil containing it is permissible...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter 'باب المتفرقات' (Chapter of Miscellaneous Matters) from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The commentator first explains the chapter title: it contains rulings that do not fit neatly into the previous chapters, and different manuscripts call it 'Miscellaneous Questions' or 'Scattered Questions'. The main ruling stated is that the sale of dogs, leopards, other predators, and birds (trained for hunting) is valid (sahih). This is a ruling statement supported by evidence. The proof is twofold: (1) A report from Abu Hanifa that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted taking a price for a hunting dog; (2) Logical reasoning: the dog is valuable property (māl mutaqawwim) used as a tool for hunting, so its sale is like the sale of a falcon (bāzī). Since the Lawgiver allowed its use for guarding and hunting, sale is also allowed because sale is merely a transfer of ownership of a beneficial object. The commentator then addresses a key issue: the status of the dog's najāsah (ritual impurity). According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) opinion, the dog is pure (tāhir) in essence. This is different from the pig, which is impure in essence. Even if one accepts the narration that the dog is impure (some Hanafi transmissions), that would only forbid eating it, not selling it. The prohibition of selling an impure thing is because its benefit is prohibited, but the benefit of a dog (hunting/guarding) is permitted, so sale is allowed. The commentator compares to dung (sarqīn) and livestock droppings (ba'r) which are impure but can be sold because their benefit is permitted, unlike human waste (adhirah) which has no permitted benefit. The passage ends by discussing a hypothetical where if impurity mixed with soil becomes permissible by consumption (like impure oil burned for light), then the sale of that soil would be allowed. This shows the Hanafi principle that if an impure object has a permitted use, its sale is valid.

Note: The linguistic discussion of 'tast' (a container) is an interjection from the commentator and is not related to the fiqh ruling on dogs. It appears because of the copyist or as a note.

Key Terms
كلب (kalb): Dog. Specifically, a hunting dog or guard dog that has permitted benefit.
سباع (sibāʿ): Predators such as leopards, lions, etc., that are trained for hunting or keeping. The text implies they can be sold if useful.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection (dhimmah) of the Islamic state.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is lawful to use and trade in the Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the key factor that makes the sale of a dog valid?
A) The dog is considered pure (tāhir).
B) The dog is a useful animal for hunting or guarding.
C) The dhimmi can sell it to a Muslim.
D) The hadith about cursing the price of a dog is weak.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling dogs, predators, and birds because they have permitted benefit and are considered pure, unlike wine and pigs which are absolutely prohibited.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Validity of Selling Hunting Dogs and Predatory Animals
Ruling on sale of dogs and predatory animals with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of hunting dogs and predators, identify the proofs (hadith and qiyas), and explain why impurity does not prohibit sale.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits selling hunting dogs because they are valuable property with permissible benefit.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common question: can Muslims sell dogs and predatory animals? While many assume dogs are impure and thus unsellable, the Hanafi school holds a nuanced position. The matn states that the sale of dogs, cheetahs, predators, and birds is valid, and the commentary provides the legal reasoning—a hadith permission and an analogy to falcons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the matn: 'It is valid to sell the dog, the cheetah, the predators, and birds, and the dhimmi is like the Muslim in selling other than wine and pig.' Note that the ruling applies to dhimmis as well, except for wine and pig.
The focus here is on animals used for hunting or guarding.
2
Examine the primary dalil naqli
The commentator cites a hadith narrated by Abu Hanifa: 'He (the Prophet) permitted the price of a hunting dog.' This is a direct proof of permissibility.
The word 'رخص' indicates permission.
3
Follow the qiyas
The text draws an analogy to a falcon (bazi): since it is a tool for hunting and therefore valuable property, its sale is valid. Similarly, a hunting dog shares the same effective cause (illa): being a valuable tool for hunting.
The illa is 'being a mal mutaqawwim and a hunting tool.'
4
Address the counter-argument: impurity
Even if the dog's substance is impure (a minority narration), that does not prohibit sale. Impurity only prohibits eating, not sale, as long as there is permissible benefit. The text compares it to dung, which is impure but still sold for beneficial use.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that the dog's substance is pure.

Chapter of Miscellaneous Issues: It is valid to sell the dog, the cheetah, the predators, and birds, and the dhimmi is like the Muslim in selling other than wine and pig, even... In al-Sihah: al-Tast is al-Tass in the language of Tayy, substituting a taa for one of the two sins for heaviness; when you pluralize or diminutive, you return the sin because you separate them with an alif or ya; you say tisas and tisis. And al-qamqama with damm is known. Al-Asma'i said: it is Roman; plural qamaqim; like in al-Sihah. End. Allah knows best.
Thus, Chapter of Miscellaneous Issues, al-Hidayah, in the copy of al-Zaylai. In the copy of al-Ayni: 'Miscellaneous Issues' and he called them 'Scattered Issues'; the meaning is the same. Their summary is that issues that slip away from the previous chapters and were not mentioned there, when they are collected, they are called by this name, i.e., scattered from the chapters or scattered from their chapters. His saying: (It is valid to sell the dog, the cheetah, the predators, and the birds) because Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, narrated that he permitted the price of a hunting dog, and because it is valuable property as a tool for hunting, so its sale is valid like the falcon, by the evidence that the Lawgiver permitted its use for guarding and hunting, so likewise its sale. This is according to the fatwa-giving view of the purity of its substance, unlike the pig which is impure in substance. As for the narration that it is impure in substance like the pig, in Fath al-Qadir it is said: Even if its impurity is conceded, that necessitates the prohibition of eating it, not the prohibition of its sale. Rather, the prohibition of sale is due to the prohibition of benefit by Shariah. That is why we permit the sale of dung (sarqin) and animal droppings (ba'r) despite their impurity, because benefit from them is unrestricted in our view, unlike human waste which has no unrestricted benefit, so its sale is prohibited. If it is established by Shariah that there is unrestricted benefit in it mixed with soil, even by consumption (istihlak) like using impure oil for a lamp as has been said, then the sale of that soil in which it is contained is permitted.
He erred: al-Kamal Basha in his treatise al-Maghrib, and the Imam al-Mutarrizi made a mistake in it where he said al-Tast is feminine and it is Persian, and al-Tass is its arabicization, because al-Tass is a contraction of al-Tast just as al-Tash is a contraction of al-Tasht. Likewise, al-Jawhari: his saying that al-Tast is Arabic originally al-Tass in the language of Tayy, substituting a taa for one of the two sins for heaviness; when you pluralize or diminutive you return the sin because you separate them with an alif or ya; so you say tisas and tisis. He was followed by the author of al-Qamus where he said: al-Tast is al-Tass, substituting a taa for one of the two sins. And the author of al-Mujmal also was heedless of its arabicization where he said: al-Tass is a language in al-Tast. End.

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the permissibility of selling dogs, leopards, predators, and birds, and the status of dhimmis in such sales. The commentary begins by quoting the matn: 'It is valid to sell the dog, the cheetah, the predators, and birds, and the dhimmi is like the Muslim in selling other than wine and pig.' The commentator then provides proofs. The first proof is a hadith narrated by Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) that he permitted receiving payment for a hunting dog. The second proof is qiyas: since a dog is valuable property and a tool for hunting, its sale is valid just like that of a falcon. The underlying reasoning is that the Lawgiver has permitted benefit from dogs for guarding and hunting, so sale is also permitted. The commentator notes that this ruling is based on the fatwa-giving view that the dog's substance is pure, unlike the pig which is impure. However, if one takes the narration that the dog's substance is impure, then the commentator explains from Fath al-Qadir that impurity only prohibits eating, not sale. The prohibition of sale arises only if the object has no permissible benefit in Shariah. For example, we permit the sale of dung (sarqin) and animal droppings (ba'r) despite their impurity because benefit is unrestricted. Human waste is not permitted because there is no unrestricted benefit; however, if it is mixed with soil and used for fuel like impure oil, then its sale may be permitted. The passage then includes a long lexical note about the word 'tast' (a basin), which is a digression but part of the original text. The note discusses whether it is Arabic or Persian, and some scholars' errors. Overall, the ruling is that the sale of hunting animals is valid, and dhimmis are treated like Muslims except for wine and pig.

Key Terms
كلب (kalb): A dog; specifically a hunting dog in this context.
فهد (fahd): A cheetah or leopard; a predatory animal used for hunting.
سباع (siba'): Predatory animals, including lions, wolves, etc.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and permissible to benefit from.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the primary evidence for the validity of selling a hunting dog?
A) Consensus of the Companions
B) A narration from Abu Hanifa that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted its price
C) Qiyas comparing the dog to a pig
D) The principle that all sales are valid unless specifically prohibited

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the sale of hunting dogs and predatory animals because they are valuable property with permissible benefit, supported by Prophetic permission and qiyas.

Salam in Wine and Pig: Hanafi Ruling and Proof
Ruling on salam in wine and pig

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam in wine and pig and explain the reasoning based on the specific hadith.

Core Idea
Salam in wine is valid; salam in pig is invalid because salam in live animals is not permitted.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding salam (forward sale) in two prohibited commodities: wine and pig. While the sale of wine is generally prohibited by a specific hadith, the Hanafi school permits salam in wine based on a distinct legal reasoning. For pig, the ruling is straightforward: salam is invalid because it involves a live animal, and salam in live animals is not permitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage discusses salam in wine and salam in pig. Determine the ruling for each.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Salam in wine is valid (sahih); salam in pig is invalid (batil).
The ruling for pig is based on a general principle, not on the specific prohibition of pig meat.
3
Locate the proof for wine
The prohibition of selling wine is attributed to a specific hadith: 'The One who prohibited drinking it prohibited selling it.'
The hadith is ahad, with zanni thubut.
4
Understand the reasoning for pig
Salam in live animals is invalid; pig is a live animal, so salam is invalid.
This is a standard rule in salam contracts.
5
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some schools may not permit salam in wine due to the general prohibition of selling wine.
The Hanafi school maintains its position based on the specific text and the nature of salam.

Actually the matn says salam is permitted in wine. Buying Pottery Animals for Children: Validity and Liability
Ruling on purchase of pottery animals for children's entertainment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on the validity of buying pottery animals for children and apply the condition that the item must be mal mutaqawwim for the sale to be valid.

Core Idea
A sale of an item with no permissible benefit is invalid; whether a pottery animal has benefit is disputed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a difference within the Hanafi school regarding the purchase of pottery animals for children's amusement. The ruling hinges on whether such items are considered valuable property (mal) with a legitimate purpose. Al-Qunya holds the purchase invalid, while al-Tab considers it valid. Understanding this issue helps clarify the conditions for a valid sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
State what al-Qunya and al-Tab say: al-Qunya: the purchase is invalid and the destroyer is not liable; al-Tab: the purchase is valid and the destroyer is liable.
This is a clear intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
Analyze the basis of each view
Al-Qunya likely considers the pottery animal to have no permissible benefit (only amusement), so it is not mal mutaqawwim. Al-Tab may see some benefit (e.g., decoration) making it valid property.
The key concept is mal mutaqawwim: property that is valuable and permissible to use.
3
Consider the condition for a valid sale
In Hanafi fiqh, the object of sale must be mal mutaqawwim. If the item has no permissible benefit, the sale is fasid (invalid) and the item is not legally protected property.
This is the underlying condition.
4
Apply the difference to liability
If the sale is invalid, the buyer does not own the item, so destroying it does not create liability for damages. If valid, the buyer owns it and the destroyer must compensate.
Liability follows validity.
5
Note the absence of tarjih
The commentator does not specify which view is mufta bihi, leaving both as valid positions within the school.
In practice, the mufti may choose based on circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : اشترى ثوراً أو فرساً من خزف لاستئناس الصبي لا يصح ولا يضمن متلفه (طب) صح ويضمن متلفه .
And in al-Qunya: If one buys an ox or a horse made of pottery for the child's amusement, it is not valid, and the one who destroys it is not liable. In al-Tab: it is valid, and the one who destroys it is liable.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding the purchase of pottery animals intended for children's entertainment. According to al-Qunya (a Hanafi work), such a purchase is not valid because the item has no permissible benefit; it is merely for amusement. Therefore, if someone destroys it, they are not liable for its value because it is not considered property (mal). However, according to al-Tab (likely a different source or edition), the purchase is valid, and the destroyer is liable. The commentator notes this difference without explicit tarjih. The ruling hinges on whether the pottery animal is considered valuable property. If it has no benefit, it is not mal, so the sale is invalid. If it can be used for some benefit (e.g., decoration), it may be valid.

Key Terms
خزف (khazaf): Pottery or earthenware.
متلف (mutlif): The one who destroys the item; liable for damages if item is considered property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Conditions for the Sale of Pigeon Dung
Ruling on selling pigeon dung and the minimum price for a valid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid sale of pigeon dung and identify which forms of payment invalidate the sale.

Core Idea
A sale of pigeon dung is valid only if abundant and the price is at least a fals.

The sale of seemingly worthless items can be valid if they have a lawful use and are exchanged for a recognized price. This passage from the Hanafi tradition addresses the sale of pigeon dung and establishes clear conditions: the dung must be abundant, and the price must be a standard monetary unit like a fals. Understanding these conditions helps avoid invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The passage rules on the sale of pigeon dung. The initial verdict is 'it is permitted' (يجوز).
The ruling is a hukm taklifi: permissibility (ibaha).
2
Extract the first condition: abundance
The text states 'if it is abundant' (إن كان كثيراً). This means the dung must be in a quantity that makes it useful (e.g., as fertilizer).
Scarcity may render the item valueless or negligible.
3
Extract the second condition: minimum price
The passage specifies that the minimum value (أدنى القيمة) required for a valid sale is a fals (a small copper coin). This is the minimum price threshold.
A fals is a recognized unit of currency in classical Hanafi literature.
4
Recognize the invalidating factor
The text contrasts: 'even if it were a piece of bread, it is not permitted' (ولو كانت كسرة خبز لا يجوز). Bread is not a recognized price, so using it invalidates the sale.
The price must be mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) commonly used as a medium of exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز بيع خرء الحمام إن كان كثيراً وهبته أدنى القيمة التي تشترط لجواز البيع فلس ولو كانت كسرة خبز لا يجوز ا هـ .
It is permitted to sell pigeon dung if it is abundant, and its gift [i.e., the minimum value required for the validity of sale] is a fals, and even if it were a piece of bread, it is not permitted. End.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage discusses the sale of pigeon dung. The ruling states that it is permitted to sell pigeon dung if it is abundant (kathiran), as it has a permissible use (e.g., fertilizer). However, if it is a small quantity, the sale may not be valid. The commentator also mentions the minimum value required for a sale to be valid: it must be at least a fals (a small copper coin). Even if the price were a piece of bread, the sale would not be valid because bread is not considered a standard monetary price. This is a condition for the validity of the sale: the price must be something that is considered property with value. A piece of bread is not commonly used as a price, so the sale is invalid.

Key Terms
خرء الحمام (khur' al-hamam): Pigeon droppings; considered useful fertilizer.
فلس (fals): A small copper coin; the minimum price for a valid sale in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Sale of Predators: Trainability as a Criterion (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on selling predators based on trainability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on selling predatory animals and distinguish between trainable and untrainable animals, citing the intra-madhhab dispute on monkeys.

Core Idea
Trainability determines permissibility: trainable predators are salable; untrainable ones and monkeys are not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents Abu Yusuf's ruling on selling dangerous predators. The key factor is whether the animal can be trained for a permissible benefit like hunting. Abu Yusuf clarifies that lions, cheetahs, and falcons may be sold if trainable, but leopards are never trainable. A dispute arises regarding monkeys: some permit sale for skin, but the relied-upon view prohibits it due to common misuse for amusement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
Recognize that the passage deals with the validity of selling dangerous animals (aquur). Abu Yusuf's position is that such sales are permissible only if the animal can be trained for a permissible benefit.
The default assumption in Hanafi law is that sale of animals is allowed if they have a permissible benefit.
2
Apply the trainability criterion to each animal
Go through each animal: dog, lion, cheetah, falcon, leopard, monkey. Determine if it is trainable (qubul al-ta'leem). Cheetahs and falcons are always trainable; lions and dogs depend on individual trainability; leopards are never trainable.
Trainability is the key condition for permissible benefit.
3
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute on monkeys
Note two narrations: one permits sale because the skin can be used, and another prohibits because the common purpose is amusement (haram). The commentator notes that permissibility was the more correct view based on skin/bone benefit, but al-Bada'i corrected it to prohibition.
This shows how later Hanafi authorities refined the position based on customary practice (urf).
4
State the mufta bihi position
The passage concludes that the sale of untrainable predators and monkeys is not permissible. Abu Yusuf's view is considered correct, and al-Bada'i's correction on monkeys is upheld.
This is an example of tarjih within the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن أبي يوسف منع بيع العقور وذلك في المبسوط أنه لا يجوز بيع الكلب العقور الذي لا يقبل التعليم وقال : هذا هو الصحيح من المذهب قال : وهكذا نقول في الأسد إذا كان يقبل التعليم ويصطاد به أنه يجوز بيعه وإن كان لا يقبل التعليم والاصطياد به لا يجوز . قال : والفهد والبازي يقبلان التعليم فيجوز بيعهما على كل حال .ا.هـ. فعلى هذا لا يجوز بيع النمر بحال لأنه لشراسته لا يقبل التعليم. وفي بيع القرد روايتان وجه رواية الجواز وهو الأصح كما ذكره الشارح أنه يمكن الانتفاع بجلده وهذا هو وجه إطلاق رواية بيع الكلب والسباع فإنه مبني على أن كل ما يمكن الانتفاع بجلده أو عظمه يجوز بيعه وصحح في البدائع عدم الجواز لأنه لا يشتري للانتفاع بجلده عادة بل للتلهي به وهو حرام ا هـ.
And from Abu Yusuf: prohibition of selling the dangerous predator, and that is in al-Mabsut: it is not permissible to sell the dangerous dog that cannot be trained, and he said: this is the correct position in the school. He said: And we say likewise regarding the lion: if it can be trained and used for hunting, then its sale is permissible; if it cannot be trained and used for hunting, then it is not permissible. He said: The cheetah and the falcon accept training, so their sale is permissible in any case. End. Accordingly, the sale of the leopard is not permissible in any case because it is too ferocious to be trained. Regarding the sale of the monkey, there are two narrations: the aspect of permissibility is the more correct one, as the commentator mentioned, because one can benefit from its skin. This is the reason for the absolute narration regarding the sale of dogs and predators: it is based on the principle that whatever can be benefited from by its skin or bone, its sale is permissible. However, in al-Bada'i, the lack of permissibility is corrected because it is not usually purchased for benefit from its skin, but rather for amusement, which is prohibited. End.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of predatory animals. The primary authority is Abu Yusuf, who distinguishes between trainable and non-trainable animals. In al-Mabsut, he states that a dangerous dog that cannot be trained cannot be sold. Similarly, lions are evaluated: if trainable for hunting, sale is valid; if not, invalid. Cheetahs and falcons are always trainable, so their sale is always valid. Based on this, a leopard is never trainable due to its ferocity, so its sale is never valid. For monkeys, there are two narrations: one permits sale because the skin can be used, and the other prohibits because the common purpose is amusement, which is haram. The commentator notes that the permissibility view is based on the principle that if an animal's skin or bone can be benefited from, then its sale is valid. However, al-Bada'i corrects this, stating that monkeys are not usually bought for skin but for entertainment, so their sale is not valid. Thus, the relied-upon position in the school (as per al-Bada'i) is that monkey sale is invalid. This passage illustrates how the Hanafi scholars use the criterion of permissible benefit to determine the validity of sales.

Key Terms
عقور (aquur): A dangerous animal that attacks; specifically one that cannot be trained.
قبول التعليم (qubul al-ta'leem): Capability of being trained; condition for permitted sale of predators.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, if trained
B) Yes, always
C) No
D) Only for its skin

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits sale of predators only if they are trainable for a permissible benefit; the sale of monkeys is invalid due to customary amusement.
